MAP   IBRD 23398 Malawi Power V Project Dated December 1991
This report is based en the findings of an appraisal mission which
visited Malawi in August/September 1991. The appraisal mission was led
by Mr. Assefa Telahun, Sr. Power Engineer (AP6IE). Mr. Robin
Broadfield, Sr. Energy Economist (AP6IE) and Nr. Gulam Dhalla
(Coneultant, Financial Analyst) were the additional mission members.
Valuable advice was received from Messrs. John Besant-Jones, Edwin Moore
(IENED), John Graves (APTEF) and Robert Tillman (AFTEN). Secretarial
Gupport was provided by Mrs. Dotilda Sidibe. Messrs. David Cook (AF6TE)
and Stephen Denning (AF6DR) are the managing Division Chief and
Department Director.



NAIAWI
POWER V PROJECT
CREDIT AND PROJECT SUNMARY
Borrower:            Government of Malawi
Amount:               SDR39.2 million (US$51 million equivalent)
Beneficiaries:       Electricity Supply Commission of Malawi (ESCOM)I the
Department of Economic Planning and Development
(EPD); the Petroleum Control Commission (WCC); the
Tobacco Control Commission (TCC).
Terms:               IDA standard terms, maturity of 40 years including
10 years grace.  Onlending of US$53.75 million to
ESCOM for the power component at 7.23% interest
rate, repayment period of 20 years including 5 years
grace, ESCOM bearing the foreign exchange risk. The
balance will be passed on to EPD (US$600,000), PCC
(US$250,000) and TCC (US$400,000).
Pr'-ject Objectives:  The objective of the proposed Project is to assist
the Government to implement the priority components
of the least cost power development program.  The
subsidiary objectives are to improve the quality of
power supply and to strengthen energy sector
institutions to plan and manage energy sector
activities at sectoral and sub-sectoral levels.
Project Description: The power component of the Project would comprise:
(i) increasing the hydro generation capacity by 50MW
through the construction of the f4rst phase of a
hydroelectric power plant at Kapichira Falls,
including related transmission system expansion, and
engineering services for design and supervision;
(ii) rehabilitation of the existing hydro, standby
gas turbine and diesel power plants to improve their
availability; (iii) reinforcing and expanding the
transmission and distribution systems, to improve
reliability,   reduce   losses   and   connect   new
consumers; (iv) strengthening ESCOM through the
provision of couputer hardware and software, studies
and training.  The non-power component would: (i)
assist the Government (EPD) in energy planning and
policy formulation through provision of technical
assistance; (ii) support the PCC in least cost
petroleum  product   sourcing  through  technical
assistance; and (iii) provide technical assistance
to the TCC in improving barn design for smallholder
tobacco curing, to reduce woodfuel consumption.
Benefits and Risks:  The Project would enhance the economic utilization
of indigenous energy resources by increasing hydro
generation capability and by reducing system losses



- ii -
and improving system reliability. The Project also
supports energy efficiency initiatives which will
have  marked  influence  in  reducing  woodfuel
consumption (by an average of at least 30%) in the
tobacco curing industry, second only to households
in  woodfuel  consumption.    Malawi's  import  of
petroleum products is draining a substantial
percentage (currently about 16X) of its foreign
currency earnings. The Project supports efforts to
reduce import and delivery prices of petroleum
products. The estimated economic rate of return of
the power component of the Project is 16.1Z, which
is above the opportunity cost of capital in Malawi.
The Project runs the risk of delay if the financing
gap   for   the   transmission   and   distribution
reinforcement subcomponents cannot be closed as
early  as possible.    The  financing  gap  can be
eliminated   by trimming the Project to only the
hydroelectric power plant.   However,  this would
jeopardize some of the objectives and benefits of
the Project. Trimming may also adversely affect the
interest of possible cofinanciers. Dated covenants
in the legal documents are included to help minimize
this risk.   ESCOM, with the assistance of the
Government, will otherwise be able to finance the
transmission   and   distribution   reinforcement
components.     There is also the risk that new
generation capacity may be commissioned too early or
too late if demand forecasts prove inaccurate or are
adversely  affected  by  international  economic
situations or unforeseen political developments in
the region.   The power scheme as studied has no
complex structures and will have no significant
environmental  impact;  no  technical  risks  are
therefore  anticipated.    The  financial  risk  of
project costs exceeding the estimates is minimized
through independent cost updates of the hydro
component of the Project.   The transmission and
distribution components could be reduced in scope if
costs increase, without substantially altering their
specific objectives.

I. THE ENERGY SECTOR
Economic Background
1.1         Malawi is a small, landlocked, predominantly rural country of about
8.5 million inhabitants, bordered by Mozambique to the south-west and south-east,
Zambia to the north-west and Tanzania to the north-east.   The bulk of the
population is engaged in small-scale agriculture, and average per capita income
is a low US$190 per year (1990). Major exports are tobacco, tea and sugar.
1.2   After a period of healthy economic expansion in the 1970s, declining export
earnings and war in Mozambique slowed 3conomic growth in the 1980's below the
population growth of 3.5%. Increases in smallholder output and increased balance
of payments support helped stimulate economic recovery in 1988-91, when economic
growth averaged close to 5% per annum. Given reasonably stable export earnings
and sustained donor assistance, annual growth in the 4 - 4 1/2% range is
projected for che first half of the 1990s; however, the ongoing draught will
adversely effect the expected economic growth.
Energy Resources
1.3         Malawi's major energy resource is biomass, which, in the form of wood
and charcoal, meets the bulk of household and a large proportion of agricultural
energy needs. Several rivers, notably the Shire, which drains from Lake Malawi
into the Zambezi, have hydropower potential.   The country also has modest
reserves of generally low-quality coal. Despite active, no sources of oil or gas
have been discovered. Detailed information on the country's energy resources is
contained in the joint UNDP/World Bank Energy Assessment Report (Malawi: Issues
and Options in the Energy Sector, April 1982).
1.4         Biomass.  Trees and shrubs cover about 4 million hectares, 352 of
Malawi's total land area. Indigenous vegetation on customary land accounts for
about 75% of the 4 million hectares, forest reserves for about 20%, Government
and private plantations, national parks and game reserves for the balance.
Average annual sustainable wood yield is low, due to the slow-growing character
of indigenous stock and generally poor ecological conditions.  In aggregate,
sustainable biomass supply is estimated to be greater than current national
consumption. However, there is a serious regional imbalance between supply and
demand. Roughly 50% of supply is located in the sparsely populated Northern
Region.   In the Central and Southern Regions in which nearly 90Z of the
population live, land clearing for agriculture and wood use for fuel and
construction is rapidly exhausting existing biomass resources, particularly on
customary land. Recent estimates suggest that these resources are shrinking by
about 3.52 per year and that supplies from customary land in the south and parts
of the center could be nearing exhaustion in less than ten years.
1.5         Sugar cane molasses and juices produced by Malawi's two sugar estates
are used to produce ethanol, which is blended with petrol to reduce petroleum
imports. The ethanol plant, which was commissioned in 1982, currently produces
an average of about 10 million liters of ethanol per year, sufficient to achieve
an average ethanol/petrol blend of about 15:85. Consideration is being given to
the establishment of a second ethanol plant. However, the maximum feasible
ethanol/petrol blend that can be used without costly engine conversion is about
20:80, so full utilization of this additional supply would require the conversion



-2-
of some vehicles to 1001 ethanol operation. The economics of this project should
therefore be studied carefully before a decioion l.s made.
1.6         Coal. roal deposits occur in several different locations in Malawi.
The most promising deposits in terms of quality and recoverability are near Ngana
in the remote north of the country, where estimated reserves are in region of 16
million tons. However, high transport costs to the distant markets in the center
and particularly the south have rendered exploitation of these reserves
uneconomic. Other deposits are generally in geologically distarbed areas, where
limited seam thickness and poor quality severely limit their economic potential.
Mining began in 1985 at Kaziwiziwi, a small reserve in the north. About 11S,000
tonnes of coal has been produced from Kaziwiziwi, but accessible reserves are now
virtually depleted. Production has recently switched to neighboring Mchenga,
which is now meeting the bulk of demand. With support from the Government of
France, exploratory drilling program has been completed at two sites in the
south, Lengwe and Mwabvi. The reaults of the drilling program suggest that, on
average, the coals have a relatively low calorific value and high ash content.
However, some areas of the Mwab'i deposit are potentially promising, and further
exploratory investigation has been recommended and is planned.
1.7         HydroDower.   The hydropower potential of Malawi (Annex 1.1) is
concentrated on the Shire river, which flows south from Lake Malawi and joins the
Zambezi in Mozambique. The Shire's estimated hydro potential is about 600 MW and
3,500 GWh. To date, 144 MW has been developed. Several smaller rivers, such as
the Songwe, South Rukura, Wovwe and Bua, have limited hydro potential at a number
of small sites, estimated to total about 300-400 MW. The development of a 4 MW
mini-hydro plant on the Wovwe river is planned as a first step towards
exploitation of this resource.
1.8         Other Energy Resources.  The average insolation level in Malawi is
a relatively high 18MJme.  However, the only significant use of solar energy is
by the Department of Posts and Telecommunicat-Ions and the Malawi Broadcasting
Corporation for communications, using solar photo-voltaLc panels. There is also
very limited use of wind for water pumping in isolated areas.
Energy Demand and Supply
1.9         Malawi's primary energy consumption totalled an estimated 2.43
million tonnes of oil equivalent (toe) in 1989. Woodfuels accounted for 93%,
petroleum products for 4%, electricity for 2% and coal and ethanol for the
remaining 1%. Average per capita energy consumption is a low 0.36 toe per year.
Households consume about 85Z of total final energy, agriculture 8%, transport 4%,
manufacturing 1.5% and assorted commercial activities the remainder. An energy
balance for 1989 is shown in Annex 1.2 and the pattern of final energy
consumption summarized in Table 1.1 below.
1.10     Woodfuels. An estimated 8.8 million tonnes of wood was used for energy
purposes in 1989, about 94% of which was consumed by households, 5% in the
processing of tobacco and tea and the balance in small rural and urban
industries, such as brick making and fish smoking. Rural households gather wood
free of charge for cooking and space heating purposes from neighboring trees,
bushes and shrubs. They also use crop residues when these are available and/or
wood is scarce. Most urban households use a combination of wood and charcoal.



-3-
The charcoal and some of the wood io purchased, the remainder of the wood
gathered. The tobacco and tea industries use wood either from their estates or
neighboring customary land for curing and barn cor.struction.

Rapid population growth., now over 3.5% per year, relatively high population
densities, competition for land from agriculture and the influx of nearly one
million refugees from ozasmbique have combined to cause rapid depletion of wood
resources in many parts of the southern and central regions.
1.11     With IDA and other donors' support, the Government has taken a number
of initiatives to expand woodfuel supply and slow demand growth. During the
19800, about 20,000 hectares of eucalyptus plantations were established by the
Forestry Department. However, due to a combination of poor soils, inadequate
maintenance and termite damage, their average annual yield has been under
5m3/ha, less than 50% of target, and costs per unit have consequently been much
higher than forecast.  Reflecting this experience, the major emphasis of wood
supply policy has now shifted to improving the management of existing resources
and encouraging private tree planting. Under the ongoing IDA Energy I project,
the Vorestry department has expanded tree seedling production from its network
of nurseries, introduced a tree planting incentive payment sastem for private
farmers, and io expanding its extension, protection and wood revenue collection
activities.
1.12     Demand-side initiatives have focussed on improving the efficiency of
wood use in the tobacco industry, the largest commercial user of woodfuels,
primarily through the introduction of an energy-saving furnace design. This has
contributed to a dramatic reduction in wood consumption from 42m3 per tonne of
tobacco cured in 1984 to 30-20m3 per tonne in 1989, with consequential
environmental benefit. Tobacco curers are also being encouraged to use softwood
charcoal produced from the thinnings of the large Viphya pine forest in place of
traditional hardwood, with promising succees to date at larger estate farms. In
line with IDA's policy to diversify away from tobacco production (OD 4.76',
Malawi is pursuing a research-oriented approach, with which IDA agrees, to
enhance the production of alternative crops (cotton, soya beans, macademia nuts,
ground nuts, horticultural produce etc.). However, IDA also recognizes the need
for flexibility in applying the tobacco policy to a country like Malawi, which
is heavily dependent on tobacco for its foreign currency earnings. The proposed
Project therefore complements the activities under Energy I by providing
technical assistance for improved barn design for communal tobacco curing by
smallholders (paras. 2.25 and 4.11H) and encouraging the use of softwood



.4 -
charcoal.   In an effort to reduce household wood consumption, the Energy I
Project is introducing more efficienc charcoal cooking stoves, initially in
Blantyre and later in Lilongwe and Mzuzu.  They are based on a Kenyan design and
qan produce fuel savings of 30-45%. Several thousand have been manufactured by
local artisans and sold through hardware stores in Blantyre.
1.13     In an effort to discourage charcoal productior. from indigenous trees
oA customary land and stimulate sale of charcoal from the Viphya Forest and other
forest reserves, the Government has recently prohibited the sale of hardwood
charcoal. However, the long transport distance to market and low density of
softwood charcoal from the Viphya have resulted in deterioration during
transport, high delivered cost and red'c. i combustion efficiency, v,hich have
dampened consumer interest in the softw  t alternative.  Achievement of the
Energy I Project's objecti-te of commercia.-.4zing the charcoal production and
marketinp is being Impeded due to these difficulties.   The Government hae,
however, taken the positive step of legalizing the licensing of private charcoal
production from the Viphya by the Forestry Department.   The Viphya Wood
Corporation, in collaboration with the Forestry Department, is also investigating
the briquetting of softwood charcoal to reduce transport damage.
1.14     Petroleum Products.  Con*numption of petroleum products, all of which
are imported in refined form, totalled about 150 million liters in 1989. Prior
to the outbreak of civil war in Mozambique, the products were imported through
the Mozambique port of Beira, the nearest deep-water port to the main consumption
centers in Malawi.  During the 1980s, insecurity in Mozambique forced Malawi to
diversify its source of supply. In 1989, just over half (54%) of supplies were
procured from the Republic of South Africa via Zimbabwe, 20% from Dar-es-Salaam
in Tanzania, 13% from the Ndola refinery in Zambia and 12% from Beira. The
country has product stora&e capacity equivalent to three weeks consumption of
diesel and petrol, two weeks of paraffin and tw¢ months of jet fuel. Concerned
about this relatively small storage volume, the Government is planning to
construct 10,000m3 of additional petrol and diesel storage capacity, equivalent
to a'further three weeks of consumption.
1.15     The bulk of procurement is handled by the Industry Petroleum Supplies
Unit (IPSU), which is operated by OILCOM, one of four petroleum companies active
in the country. The purchases are usually made through OILCOM's international
shareholder, BP. The recently-established Petroleum Control Commission (PCC),
a public oversight body, has arranged some small-volume purchases from
neighboring Zambia and Zimbabwe and has recently tendered for a bulk shipment in
order to better assess the cost-effectiveness of the current procurement system.
With the exception of one storage depot owned by the Government for strategic
reasons, the major storage facilities are all owned and operated by the four
companies.   They also handle all petroleum wholesaling,  distribution and
retailing. OILCOM, the largest, has about 55% of the market, Mobil 22%, Caltex
15Z and Total 7Z. The investigations of the PCC, the preliminary findings of the
Energy Pricing study prepared under the Energy I Project and a review of
petroleum procurement procedures by the appraisal mission suggest there is
potential to reduce the cost of petroleum procurement, storage and bulk marketing
through the introduction of more competition into the process. The PCC is the
organ4zation currently best placed and equipped to inject that competition. The
proposed project therefore provides technical assistance to the PCC to examine
tha scope for and means by which it could reduce the cost of petroleum supply,



-5-
and for a review of its legal framework to Ider.tify any changes that would be
needed for it to play a more a4tivist role it the study suggests such a role
would be cost-effective (para. 4.11G).
1.16     Electricity.  A total of 569GWh of electricity was consumed in 19909
equivalent to about 70 kWh per capita.  Some 40,000 consumers are currently
connected to the grid, of which about 32,000 are residential consumers and most
of the remainder are small businesses. ESCOM's ten largest customers accounted
for 30% of total salee in 1990, the most important of which are Sucoma (a augar
estate) v Blantyre Water Board and David Whitehead (textiles). Small industrial,
commercial, government and agricultural consumers accounted for 52% of sales and
residential consumers for the remaining 18%. Reflecting low average household
incomes and the high proportion of rural dwellers, only about 2% of Malawian
households have access to electricity. (There are however a considerable number
of urban and periurban potential consumers presently unconnected due partly to
ESCOM's policy of requiring new customers the pay 90% of the cost of system
extensions. This is being addressed by an on-going tariff study under the Energy
I Project.) Over the period 1985-90, zonsumption grew by an average of 7.2% per
year, slightly belaw the twenty-year average growth rate of e.5%.
1.17     Coal.   Total consumption of coal in 1990 was about 53,000 tonnes,
43,000 of which was supplied from MIDCOR's two mines at Kaziwiziwi and Mchenga,
primarily the latter. The security situation and restricted access to foreign
exchange limited imports from the Moatize mine in Mozambique, the least-cost
source, to 8,000 tonnes. The major consumers are Portland Cement (33%) and David
Whitehead (23%).
Organization and Plann ng of the Energy Sector
1.18     The Ministry of Forestry and Natural Resources (MFNR) is responsible
for biomass and hydrocarbon energy resources. Biomass supply issues are dealt
with by the Forestry Department of the same ministry. The Petroleum Control
Commission (PCC) is responsible for oversight of petroleum supply and marketing,
and production and supply of coal is handled by the Mining Investment and
Development Corporation (MIDCOR)e The generation, transmission and distribution
of electricity is the responsibility of the Electricity Supply Commission of
Malawi (ESCON). PCC, MIDCOR and ESCOM are 3 of the 41 parastatals, with varying
degrees of autonomy, established by the Government to discharge those activities
which it judged could not effectively be handled by the private sector or by the
civil ser7ice. The Department of Statutory Bodies (DSB), established by the
Government in 1980 in the Office of the Prosident and Cabinet, exercises control
over the parastatals through their respective Boards. Board members include a
representative of the ministry with direct oversight responsibility for the
parastatal, the Ministry of Finance (ex-officio) and other organizations or
individuals having relevant qualifications and experience.   The oversight
ministries responsible for the energy sector are MFNR (woodfuel, petroleum
exploration, coal) and Ministry of Trade, Industry and Tourism (petroleum supply,
pow3r). Annex 2.1 shows the management of the planning process of parastatal
activities at government level.
1.19     The Energy Planning Division under the Department of the Economic
Planning and Development (EPD) is the responsible unit for energy policy
formulation and energy sector planning. This was established under the technical



-6-
assistance program of IDA's credit 1428-MAI, and continued its activity to-date
through the support provided by the UNDP Project 786/010 (executed by the Bank)
through the provision of: (i) an energy advisor; (ii) graduate studies and
training; and (iii) computer hardware and software.  The least cost power
development program (resulting in the prioritization of the Tedeani III hydro
power plant, being implemented under Energy I, and the Kapichira hydro scheme,
the main component of the proposed project) has been co-ordinateod by the Energy
Division's predecessor, the Energy Planning Unit. An Energy Plan, setting out
priorities and outlining options, has also been prepared. The Division was the
implementing unit for the Energy Pricing Study funded under the Energy I Project.
Updating of the Energy Plan on a regular basis, strengthening energy planning
activities at sub-sector and at government levels, and preparing recommendations
to Government on energy issues as they arise will continue to be the role of the
Energy Planning Division under EPD.   The proposed project provides  for
strengthening of the Division through assistance in the formulation of a
household energy strategy and provision of experts and training on as-needed
basis "para. 4.11F). Unfortunately, two high level, experienced and well-trained
economists have resigned recently from the Division to take better-paid jobs
outside the Government.   In an effort to maintain the Energy Division's
capability in energy planning and policy analysis, the appointment of at least
one additional experienced economist in the Division will be a condition of
disbursement of the proposed Power V Credit. However, the sustainability of
these functions cannot be assured in the long term unless the government is
prepared to address the issue of inadequate salaries for skilled civil servants.
Government's Enerav Sector Obiectives and Strateag
1.20     The Government's Statement of Development Policies, 1987-1996, and the
subsequent National Energy Plan for 1988-1997 set out the main objectives and
strategies for the Energy Sector in Malawi. The objectives are: (a) minimizing
the impact of the high cost and of the uncertainties in supply of imported oil
and coal; (b) developing a sustainable program to meet woodfuel and construction
needs; and (c) developing electric power supply in a least-cost manner to meet
the demands of the economic and social development of the country. To achieve
these objectives, the Government has laid out the following strategies: (a)
improving  the  procurement  and  distribntion  of  petroleum,  including  the
establishment of a national strategic res,_ve; (b) supporting the production of
ethanol for blending with petrol as transport fuel; (c) exploring for and
undertaking coal mining activity; (d) enlcouraging conservation of woodfuel
resources including conversion of forest thinnings to softwood charcoal; (e)
developing and implementing a least cost power development program; and (f)
strengthening energy sector institutions to plan, manage and operate their sub-
sectoral activities and Government's capability for energy planning and co-
ordination. The Government hae also been negotiating directly with international
oil companies to explore for hydrocarbon resources.
1.21     The Government is taking a variety of initiatives to improve the
management of wood energy resources on a cost effective and environmentally sound
basis.  Thase are being actively supported by the ongoing Energy I Project,
funded by IDA, as discussed in paragraph 1.12 above. A mid-term review of the
wood energy component of the Energy I Project, planned for 1992, will assess the
success of these initiatives and will redirect the emphasis of the project where



-7-
necessary. The mid term review is also expected to generate policy options and
strategies for future wood energy programs.
1.22     In the area of commercial energy, indigenous hydro-electricity is both
the least cost and most reliable source of supply.  Therefore a top priority of
the Government, which this project will support, is to develop the country's
hydro resources at a rate sufficient to meet demand at full cost-recovery prices.
This will ensure both adequate availability of commercial energy to support
economic development and growth and that this energy is supplied at minimum
economic cost and in a manner that minimizes the conmitment of scarce foreign
exchange. In an effort to reduce the cost of imported petroleum, the Government
has established the PCC to supervise petroleum procurement and distribution.
Coal exploration has been redirected from the north to the south, which is closer
to the main markets.  However, coal production, thtough uneconomic, is still
proceeding in the north. Ethanol blending is reducing petroleum imports by about
7%. The initial phase of the current least-cost power development program is
under implementation (para. 1.17). An Energy Pricing Study, funded under Energy
IT will assist the rationalization of pricing policies for solid and liquid
fuels. The Government's energy institution-building effort is achieving the
desired goal of more effective energy planning and co-ordination. With donor
assistance, the power utility has substantially improved its capability to plan
and manage its operations. The initiatives the Government is pursuing will be
further enhanced by the proposed project through the assistance to be provided
to ESCOM, the PCC and the EPD.
Exoerience with Past and Ongoinsa Lending
1.23     The World Bank has provided three credits and one loan for power to
Malawi. A fourth Credit, Energy I (Cr. 1990-HAI), of US$46.7 million was granted
in 1990 and is devoted partly to power (68%) and partly to wood energy. The
first IDA credit (178-MAI) for US$ 5.25 million was made in 1970 to help finance
the foreign exchange cost of the Tedzani hydroelectric power plant. The second
stage of Tedzani and a 15NW standby gas plant in Blantyre were financed with a
second IDA credit (426-MAI) of US$7.5 million and a loan of US$6 million from the
Commonwealth Developmex.t Corporation (CDC). A total amount of US$25 million was
provided in three lots to finance the civil works component of he 80MW Nkula B
hydroelectric power plant. The first lot (Loan 1387-NAI) of US$9 million was
supplemented by a second loan (1388-HAl) of US$8 million and a credit (691-NAI)
of US$8 million. CDC, the African Development Bank (AfDB) and Kreditanstalt fur
Wiederaufban  (KfW) provided US$82 million towards  the cost of Nkula B.
(Presently KfW is funding the installation of the fifth 20NW unit at Nkula B).
A Special Project Preparation Facility provided by IDA in 1986 assisted in the
preparation of the least cost power development program which formed the basis
for the ongoing Energy I Project (Cr.1990-MAI) and the proposed Power V Project.
1.24     Credit  1428-MAI  asGisted  the  Government  in  establishing  and
strengthening a planning unit in the Department of Economic Planning and
Development (presently the Energy Planning Division of EPD). Credit 992-MAI
(US$13.82 million) was the first forestry sub-sector credit given in 1980 to
assist periurban woodfuel plantation schemes. A second loan of US$16.7 million
(Loan 2670-HAI) was provided in 1986 for the Wood Energy II Project to reorient
the Government away from wood production and towards the establishment of a
policy framework to encourage private wood production, conservation and more



- 8 -
efficient utilization of existing biomass resources. The IBRD loan has since
been canceled, the remaining component of the Wood Energy II Loan reappraised,
and included as part of the Energy I Credit 1990-MAI.
1.25     The Project Completion Reports for the loans and credits (Cr. 691-MAI,
Ln.1387 and Ln.1388-MIA) listed above underscore that the main objectives of the
power projects have been achieved, and have made significant contributions to the
provision of efficient and reliable power supply in Malawi. The institutional
strengthening efforts have had a marked influence on the operation of ESCOM. The
lessons learnt emphasize, among others, the possible impact of bad rocks even on
short tunnels, the impact of transport difficulties on implementation schedules
and the influence of economic development and price increases on load forecasts.
These lessons have been reflected in the design of the on-going Energy I Project.
ESCOM staff has been trained in demand forecasting, the results of which are
reviewed regularly, taking into account the growth of the economy, industrial
consumptions and historical trends for the commercial and domestic consumers.
These are checked against long-term forecasts made by consultants in connection
with the least cost power development program. Timing of the components of the
IPPS is thus reviewed continuously.
Energv Priciin
1.26     Prices of most petroleum products (diesel, petrol and kerosene), of
electricity and of coal are set by the Government, based on proposals from the
industries concerned. Petroleum nroduct prices are based on a "deemed" landed
price, which nominally reflects the weighted average CIF border cost of products
, delivered via the different supply routes.  In the case of diesel and petrol,
taxes, duties, contributions to the Price Stabilization Fund and the paraffin
subsidy are added to the deemed CIF price. In the case of paraffin, taxes and
duties are added and the subsidy deducted.  The net effect is to reduce the
deemed CIF price for paraffin by 7.5 tambala/liter. Regulated wholesale and
retail margins, whach vary slightly between the three products, are then added
to obtain the fine! consumer prices.  Consumer prices of diesel and petrol,
respectively K2.0/litre (US$0.69/liter) and K2.3/liter (US$0.79/liter) in mid-
1991, were above the economic cost of supply. Paraffin was priced about 10%
below the cost of supply at market exchange rates. The Energy Pricing Study
prepared under the Energy I Project recommends: (a) elimination of the paraffin
subsidy, on the grounds that it principally benefits better-off families; (b)
more rigorous review of margins; (c) introduction of off-shore tendering to
compete with OILCOM procurement; and (d) the establishment of common storage, to
be available on a fee basis to any wholesaler or retailer. The Government is
currently considering these recommendations. The proposed technical assistunce
to the PCC (para 4.11 F) will examine the potential and means for the PCC to help
implement recommendations (b), (c) and (d).
1.27     Electricity  tariffs  are proposed  by ESCOM  and approved by  the
Government.    In  FY90/91,  ESCOM's  average  tariff was  tambalas  12.4/kWh
(USc4.4/kWh). While low by international standards, this was sufficient to cover
all ESCOM's operating and debt service costs and to finance 33% of capital
expenditures in that year.   Tariffs are usually increased annually at the
beginning of each fiscal year. The FY91/92 tariffs, in effect from April 1,
1991, average at tambalas 13.5/kWh (USc4.7/kWh) (Annex 5.1). A thorough tariff
study is nearing completion financed under the Energy I Project. The interim



-9-
conclusions of the study suggest that, with the exception of the "general"
cAtegory of consumers, tariffs are below the long run marginal cost of supply at
the appropriate voltage level. The study also confirms the mission's assessment
that tariffs will need to rise in real terms during the 1990s to cover the costs
of ESCOM's ambitious power investment program. ESCOM and the Government have
agreed to review and discuss with the IDA the results of the tariff study and to
begin implementation of its recommendations on tariff structure with effect from
April 1993.
1.28     Domestic  coal grices  are  proposed  by MIDCOR  and  its monopoly
transporter, AMI, and approved by Government.  With the exception of Portland
Cement, the largest consumer, MIDCOR sells exclusively through AMI at a price of
KIOO/tonne (US$35/tonne) ex-mnie in mid-1991. Portland Cement, which arranges
its own transport, was paying about K90/tonne (US$31/tonne) ex-mine. The ANI
coal price is about 25Z above current production costs, but below forecast long-
term production costs. It is 35% higher than the ex-mine cost of coal from the
Moatize mine in Mozambique, transport of which is also cheaper than Mchenga coal
to the main markets in southern Malawi. Security problems on the route from
Moatize and restricted access to foreign exchange currency allow MIDCOR/AMI to
dominate the market despite their higher prices.
II. THE BORROWER AND THE IMPLEMENTING AGENCIES
The Borrower
2.1      The Government of Malawi would be the Borrower for the proposed IDA
Credit of SDR39.2 million (US$55 million equivalent) for Power V Project.
2.2      The Government would on-lend to ESCOM US$53.75 million at the current
IBRD rate (paras 4.16) to assist in the financing of the foreign exchange costs
of the power component of the project. SDR427,000 (US$6000,000 equivalent) would
be assigned to the Department of Economic Planning and Development (4.11F),
SDR178,000 (US$250,000 equivalent) to the Petroleum Control Commission (4.11G)
and SDR285,000 (US$400,000 equivalent) to the Tobacco Control Commission (4.11H)
to assist in the strengthening of the respective institutions in planning and
managing energy related activities including research.
The Implementinp Agencies
Electricity Suppl Commission of Malawi (ESCOM)
2.3      Institutional Set MR.  ESCOM is a state owned parastatal (corporation)
established by the Electricity Act of 1963 when it took over the assets and
liabilities of the former Nyasaland Electricity Supply Commission which was
established under the Electricity Act, 1956, of the former Federation of Rhodesia
and Nyasaland. ESCOM is empowered by its Act to generate, acquire or supply
electricity. ESCOM exercises control on electricity generated by others by the
power granted to it under the Act to recommend to the minister responsible the
licensing or renewal of licenses of suppliers with an installed capacity in
excess of twenty kilowatts.



- 10 _
2.4      The Commission as established, consists of a chairman and not more than
seven members appointed for a period of two years by the minister responsible for
ESCOM (the President of the Republic of Malawi). The Secretary to the Treasury
or his representative, the Secretary for Economic Planning and Development or his
representative and the Secretary for Trade and Industry or his representative are
ex-officio members.   Presently, the seven appointed members consist of the
General Manager of the Bureau of Standards, the CDC representative in Lilongwe,
the representative of private industries (sugar, agriculture, garages ...9) the
representative of breweries and three representatives of the business community
(economic services, women's and men's business associations). Presently, the
Chairman is the Deputy Governor of the Reserve Bank of Malawi.
2.5      ESCOM reports to the Governmentt through the Department of Statutory
Bodies (DSB). At least three government departments have direct interest in the
operations of ESCOM. ESCOM's linkages with other government departments is shown
in Annex 2.1. DSB is the principal Government body responsible for overviewing
all parastatals of which ESCOM is one of Malawi's 41 parastatals. Although its
legal mandate is not gazetted, it is set up to: (i) assist parastatals in sound
financial management; and (ii) overview,guidelines related to employment,
salaries, fringe and other benefits including vehicle use and housing allowances
for parastatal employees.   ESCOM also liaises with the Ministry of Trade,
Industry and Tourism (MITT) and its Secretary is one of the ex-officio members
of ESCOM's Commissioners  (para. 2.4).   MITT is infofmed on projects under
implementation as well as on major new investments and tariff issues, and
represents ESCOM's interests at Congress and Cabinet meetings. ESCOM also works
closely with the Department of Economic Planning and Development (EPD). EPD is
responsible for ensuring the economic viability of energy projects at macro
level. ESCOM's loans require the prior approval of the Ministry of Finance.
ESCOM is not allowed to correspond directly with the World Bank. Consequently
bid documents are transmitted by ESCOM to DSB which transmitts through the
Ministry of Finance to the World Bank.  This procedure is causing delays in
procurement and the need for its relaxation is addressed in para. 4.20.
2.6      Under the present arrangement, most decisions made at Commissioners'
level are referred, through DSB, to Government authorities for final approval.
The result is long delay in decision making, severely curtailing ESCOM's
efficiency in managing its affairs.
2.7   \ The Government, recognizing the weakness of the institutional framework
undertook a studyl/, with the assistance of the Bank and UNDP, to improve the
role of DSB and introduce efficiency and accountability of parastatals. DSB
would overview corporate plans, and monitor and evaluate results.   ESCOM's
Commissioners and management would maintain the automony provided by the Act,
acting independently on administrative and operational matters, including setting
of remunerations for employees, hiring and firing, promoting and taking
appropriate disciplinary actions. EPD would continue to liaise with DSB on the
economic viability of ESCOM's major projects. The role of the parent ministry
(MITT) was expected to remain the same. ESCOM's power under its Act, such as its
power to enter into a contract, is being gradually eroded by subsequent practices
1/   Malay!: Report on Paraotatal Restructuring (January 28, 1987; No. 6543-MAI) parastatal
(self contained organization normally created by a state).



- 11 -
and legislation. These have resulted in delaying project implementation and in
decisions, impeding urgent action. Discussions on the parastatal reform program,
with concrete recommendations to improve the role and the accountability of
ESCOM's Board of Commissioners and its senior management, should be re-activated.
Specific recommendations would include reinstating ESCOM's power under the Act
on matters related to motivation, promotion, transfer, hiring and firing of
employees and on extending ESCOM's power to execute construction contracts beyond
the limit of K500,000 (US$2500000) for works and R250,000 (US$100,000) for goods
and K100,000 for services which are very low for ESCOM's operations.   This
limitation has now been gazetted.  It was agreed at negotiations to increase
ESCOM's procurement limit for mandatory review by Government to US$300,000 for
purposes of the proposed Project.
2.8      Management and StaffinA. ESCOM continues to be a well managed utility.
The post of the General Manager, filled by an expatriate since ESCOM's
establishment in 1963, has been filled by a Malawian national, who was a Deputy
General Manager, since January, 1990. The former expatriate General Manager was
acting as Chief Technical Advisor to the General Manager, carrying out a
commendable job in reviewing major project documents, from feasibility studies
to bid documents and evaluation of major bids, until his retirement in June,
1991. The organization structure of ESCOM is shown in Annex 2.2. The post of
the General Manager is approved by the Minister. (The first Malawian General
Manager passed away in May, 1991, following a sudden illness. The Deputy General
Manager is acting. Selection to fill the post was in progress during appraisal.)
The posts of the Deputy General Manager, the Chief Engineers, the Commission
Secretary and the Financial Controller are approved by the Secretary to the
Office of the President as are, since recently, the posts of the 20 mangers
reporting to the four Department Heads. The recommendations of ESCOM's Board of
Commissioners is taken into consideration in the above appointments. The post
of the Financial Controller, filled by an expatriate since ESCOM's establishment,
has also been filled by a Malawian national since January, 1991. Presently there
is only one expatriate (Switchgear Instructor) in ESCOM. However, the need for
expertise in technical areas is recognized. The project provides for training
and expert services.
2.9      ESCOM's total staff at the end of February 1991 totalled 2176,
excluding temporary labor force which varies from time to time depending on the
volume of activity. This averages 8.50Z increase annually during the last three
years. This high percentage increase is accounted for by the increase in number
of projects in which ESCOM is directly involved. ESCOM is aware of this rapid
increase and is controlling staff additions in areas other than the professional
categories. Presently there are 5 vacancies in the professional categories which
ESCOM plans to fill through promotions from within the organization and through
recruitment and training.
2.10     ESCOM has difficulties in attracting professionals and personnel with
technical skill because of the standardized salary structure for all parastatals.
The salary level is not competitive enough with the private sector.
Consequently, ESCOM hae been a training ground, particularly for accountants.
The parastatal reform program had recommended rewarding of staff in accordance
with performance. This recommendation has not, as yet, been put into effect.
ESCOM therefore reverts to the introduction of indirect benefits which could be
applied only for limited posts and professions. The Government has informed at



_ 12 -
negotiations that it is studying incentive packages to better attract and retain
staff in parastatal organizations, including ESCOM. IDA's supervision missions
will closely monitor the impact of the planned measures.
2.11     Planning and Engineerine.   ESCOM has established a Planning Unit
consisting of two engineers (one of whom is the head of the UJnit) and one
economist.    Two members were  given  intensive  on-the-job  training  at  a
consultant's headquarters in England, followed by hands on experience in carrying
out a lose reduction study in ESCOM8's system under the guidance of the
consultant. The Planning Unit is uIow equipped with PCs and is undertaking, with
no external support, demand £oreaasting and distribution system planning. With
the necessary software and the planned short term training, the Unit will soon
be in a position to do generation and transmission system planning. The Unit
should be strengthened in terms of professional staff, computers and related
facilities. The Project provides funds for training ESCOM staff and for the
acquisition of computer hardware and software (para 4.11E).
2.12     ESCOM has developed expertise in the design and construction of
distribution  lines  and  substations.    ESCOM  is  also  experienced  in  the
installation of 132 or 66kV substations and in the major rehabilitation work of
its transmission facilities. ESCOM would therefore be able to carry out in-house
transmission and distribution rehabilitation and reinforcement works assisted,
where necessary, by local contracting under its supervision.    Engineering
consultants would be used to design and supervise the major component of the
project - the Kapichira scheme.
2.13     The Government has consulted the Association on the recent appointment
of the Financial Controller. To ensure the continuation of ESCOM's capability
to manage the project and its operations, the Government should consult the
Association on appointees to senior posts. During negotiations, Agreement was
reached that the Government and ESCOM would: (i) consult with IDA on the
qualifications and experience of appointees to the posts of General Manager and
those senior positions reporting directly to the General Manager (Deputy General
Manager, Chief Engineer, Financial Controller and Commission Secretary and others
that may be introduced following possible changes on the organization structure)
and (ii) take into consideration the views expressed by the Association on the
appointments.
2.14     Qperations and Haintenance. ESCOM's physical plant, which includes the
generating stations and the transmission and distribution facilities, are all
properly maintained and competently managed.   However, the passing away of
ESCOM's General Manager combined with the retirement of the Chief Technical
Advisor has created a temporary vacuum at the professional and managerial level.
Improvements in remuneration and incentives to attract and retain qualified and
experienced staff are the key factors to resolve these problems.
2.15     Account4na and Audits.   The accounts records of ESCOM are well
maintained. Monthly financial reports are submitted to management, including
performance measured against budget by department. Customer billing and payrolls
are computerized and efficient. Cost control, which is also computerized, is
exercised by cost centers and reporting of actuals against budgets is submitted
monthly by each ESCOM station. Stores, project coste, fixed assets, payables and
the general ledger are operated on manual systems. ESCOM's finance and computer



- 13 -
staff are considering to develop fully integrated computerized system with
technical assistance support ae may be needed.
2.16     Billinsz and Collection.  Meters are normally read and billed monthly.
All customer billing is computerized and bills are prepared and billed regularly
every month.   At June 30, 1991, the value of unpaid bills in the Accounts
Receivable was equal to 63 days of revenue. This indicates a marked improvement
in performance relative to figures of 69 and 66 for FYs 89/90 and 90/91
respectively (Annex 5.19 para 5.7).
2.17     Data Processing. ESCOM's billing and payroll systems are computerized.
Attempts have been made to extend computerization to stock control and to other
financial transactions.  However, the computer unit is understaffed and key
positions in the Finance Department including the computer unit are vacant.
Effective accounting systems could not be developed without a comprehensive and
a clearly definad "user" requirements, particularly if the computerization is to
serve, as it should, the needs of other departments in addition to that of
Finance. The proposed Project provides for technical assistance to develop a
strategy and to define the extent and phasing of computerization of ESCOM's
overall operations.
2.18     The computer equipment presently installed at ESCOM's headquarters in
Blantyre consists of a main frame and a processing unit, with an additional
processor installed in Lilongwe. Under the Energy I Credit two minicomputers
were purchased for Lilongwe and Mzuzu to convert these two centers of ESCOM's
activity to data processing centers for nearby districts (Annex 4.1, ....).
Subject to the findings of the technical assistance experts (para 2.16), the
proposed Project will provide one computer system for the headquarters to replace
the existing mainframe computer which is approaching its limit in terms of
capacity and is also being superseded by more modern technology,  and 18
microcomputers for the districts to be linked to the main area centers of
Lilongwe, Mzuzu and Blantyre (para 4.11E).
2.19     Training.  ESCOM's performance in training its staff and employees is
noteworthy.  Over the years it has trained a total of over 200 employees in
various fields relevant to its activities. Most of its training is done by local
institutions including its own Training Center which caters for distribution
artisans where to-date 180 of them have been trained since its establishment in
1980.  Specialized training is provided for by sending members of its staff
abroad. There are six professionals currently studying abroad one each in human
resources development, financial accounting, telecommunication design, power
distribution and two in utility management. Six employees have returned recently
after completing higher degrees in business administration, economics, energy
management, metering and hydropower development. About 7A others have attended
short courses, local and abro&d, in the areas of relevance to ESCOM's operation
in the short span of one year. A bonding agreement is entered with the staff on
training to ensure minimum period of service upon their return.
2.20     ESCOM presently trains two accountants in Malawi and will continue
providing scholarships for its accountants at the Malawi College of Accountancy.
One accountant, doing higher degree in finance and accounting, is expected to
return shortly.



- 14 -
2.21     The objectives of ESCOM's training program euiphasize capacity building
in the areas of finance, engineering, administration and management. ESCOM needs
to match its manpower resources with the rapid growth of the system. ESCOM has
submitted to Government a comprehensive manpower development plan covering the
period  1991-1997.    The  plan  provides,  among  others,  for  graduate  and
undergraduate studies and short term specialized courses abroad in the areas of
ESCOM's operations.  The Project provides funds for training and Kapichira's
implementation will have a built-in component for on-the-job training on the
design, assembly, supervision of construction, and operation of a power facility.
ESCOM will continue to finance from its own resources training provided locally.
ESCOM's Training Center would be provided with training aids to assist in the
basic understanding of generation and transmission system operation, maintenance
and trouble shooting.
2.22     Insurance and Taxes.  ESCOM maintains insurance coverage on all assets
against the risks of breakdown of plant and equipment, fire , loss and damage for
property installed or stored or in transit as well as for liability to third
parties in accordatnce with normal practice in power utilities. The insurance
cover is satisfactory and is reviewed periodically to assure adequacy. ESCOM is
normally exempt from import duties or other levies on materials, equipment and
machinery on project financed from foreign sources but is liable for import
duties of 30% on goods financed by its own sources under its revenue budget.
ESCOM's Act does not require it to pay dividend to the Government, but is liable
for corporate tax of 40Z on its surplus.   However, with capital investment
allowances of 45-55% carried forward per year on new projects, ESCOM4 is not
expected to pay corporate tax during the lifetime of the proposed Project.
Imnlementing Agencies for Non-Power Comgonents
2.23     The Enerav Division.  The Energy Division, under EPD headed by a
Principal Secretary, is responsible for energy planning activities at Government
level.  It is headed by a Principal Economist and assisted by an expatriate
Energy Advisor who, until March 1991, was funded by a UNDP project for which the
Bank was the Executing Agency.  Under the same  project two economists did
graduate studies in USA in energy economics and returned in 1990. One has left
the Division and has been replaced by a junior economist. The Energy Advisor's
contract has expired and sources of financing a replacement is being discussed
between the Government and UNDP. The Division should be strengthened to enable
it play a leading role in energy policy formulation and co-ordination.  The
Project provides training and technical assistance on as-needed basis.  The
Division, under the guidance of EPD, will be responsible for: (i) supervising the
implementation of the energy planning and policy formulation sub-component of the
Project (para 4.11F); and (ii) the implementation of the recommendations of the
ongoing energy pricing study under Energy I. During negotiations, agreement was
reached that the Government would take the views of IDA into account and begin
implementation of the agreed recommendations on the energy pricing study not
later than April 1,1993.
2.24     The Pgtrgleum Control Cionmiision.  The Petroleum Control Commission
will be responsible for the petroleum procurement and sourcing included in the
proposed Project (para 4.11G). The PCC Act of 1986 empowers it to supervise the
procurement of petroleum products, ensure the provision of adequate oil storage
facilities, set minimum stock levels for the oil industry, and recommend changes



- 15 -
in petroleum prices including provision for petroleum stabilization fund. PCC
moritors international oil prices and the private stock level of petroleum in
Halawi. PCC also formulates an emergency fuel allocation plan in the event of
disruption of supply and aims at establishing its own strategic fuel reserve
capacity.  PCC's mandate the Act is supervisory.  The proposed Project will
review the procurement, transport and bulk storage arrangements in Malawi and
will recommend a legal framework empowering PCC to take appropriate actions to
ensure least cost petroleum supply, including direct bulk procurement and bulk
sales as well as construction and operation of bulk storage facilitUs.
Tobacco Control Commission
2.25     The Tobacco Control Commission (TCC) is a parastatal reporting to DSB.
TCC will be the implementing agency for the energy efficiency sub-component of
the proposed Project.   TCC supervisesG  among others, the Tobacco Research
Institute which, under Energy I, was assisted on energy efficiency initiatives
in tobacco curing. In 1984 tobacco curing accounted for about 23% of Malawi's
woodfuel consumption, being second only to household woodfuel consumption.
Experiments under Energy I at commercial farms have resulted in savings of about
30% in woodfuel consumption for tobacco curing. The Project provides technical
assistance to demonstrate a more efficient barn design for tobacco curing by
smallholder farmers with the main objective of moderating the excessive negative
environmental impact of inefficient woodfuel consumption (para. 4.11H).
Existing Power Facilities
2.26     Generation System.  ESCOM generates almost 99.9% of its electricity
from a cascaded group of interconnected hydroelectric power plants located on the
Shire river. Their total installed capacity is 144MW. A 15MW gas turbine plant
in Blantyre, a 5.4 MW diesel power plant in Lilongwe and a 2MW diesel power plant
in Mzuzu serve as stand-by for the interconnected system. The northern towns of
Karonga and Chitipa are supplied by diesel power plants, having a total installed
capacity of 1190kW. A rural electrification program (para. 4.2) is planned to
replace these diesel plants with a 4MW hydroelectric plant.  The generation
facilities of ESCOM are summarized in Table 2.1 below and are detailed in Annex
2.4.  The hydroelectric power plants are well maintained, and their average
availability in 1990 was 90% (Annex 2.4), decreasing below the pre-1990 average
of 92%. The diesel power plants are old and could support only peaking capacity
with provision of spares under the on-going Energy I Project. The attached map
(IBRD Map no.23398) shows the location of existing and planned power plants and
transmission facilities of ESCOM.

2.27      A number of tobacco and sugar estates and religious missions produce
their electricity for their own use.  The estimated total capacity is 26MW.
About 50% of this capacity us8a bagasse from the sugar estates. Where viable,
ESCOM is extending its distribution network to replace the captive plants. A
summary list of the private plants is given in Annex 2.4.
2.28      The availability of Nkula A was low at 82% in 1990.  This is accounted
for by planned outages to carry out repair works on the units. The 4 x 10 MW
Tedzani units are showing increased frequency of unplanned outages. This is due
to the design of the cooling water system, instability of turbine governors and
damaged voltage regulators. Runner blades, runner seals and liners are also
showing signs of deterioration. Inspection of the first 2 units of Nkula B shows
deterioration in runner seals and lines. The Project provides for technical
asoistance to audit the conditions of the turbines of Nkual A and Tedzani I Units
and to specify the measures needed to be implemented under the proposed Energy
1I Project. The proposed Project also provides for the acquisition of spare
parts for the gas turbine and standby diesel power plants (para. 4.16 B, 4.169).
2.29      Load Disnatch Center.   ESCOM's load dispatch center, stationed in
Blantyre, records and controls the generation plants and the 132/66/33kV
substations with the help of the SCADA (System Control and Data Acquisition)
system.   The existing Center will be extended to provide for the on-going
generation and transmission system expansion under the Energy I Project.
2.30      Lilongwe is a major consumption center.  It does not, however, have
an effective load control center to facilitate operations of the subtransmission
and distribution systems.   The proposed project provides installation of a
distribution center for purposes of data collection end storage and to reduce
outage times of the sub-transmission and distribution systems (micro SCADA
system) of Lilongwe (para. 4.11C).
2.31      Transmission and Distribution SYstems. ESCOM's highest transmission
voltage  is  132kV.    The  next  level  of  transmission  voltage  is  66kV.
Subtransmission is at 33kV. Distribution is effected at 11/.4kv. ESCOM's 1990
transmission system consists of 416km of 132kV and 1018 km of 66kV lines. The
subtransmission and the distribution network consists of 1538 km of 33kV and
1776km of 11 kV of overhead lines with additional 81km of 11kV underground
cables. The substation transformer capacity, excluding generation plants, totals
187.5 MVA. The corresponding figure for distribution transformer capacity is 255
MVA,  excluding 92MVA 33/11kV subtransmission capacity.   The distribution
transformer capacity is slightly under a factor of 2 (normal indicators 2 to 4)
relative to the 1990 maximum demand of 119 MW indicating the need for increasing
the distribution capacity in the existlng system.
2.32      Most of the 66kV transmission and practically all of the distribution
network use locally impregnated wooden poles.   The 265km transmission line,
designed and constructed for 132kV, presently being operated at 66kV and carrying
a peak load of about 8MW, is also constructed in impregnated poles and cross
arms. The 132kV 75km transmission line from Salima to Lilongwe has similar
features. Although the two lines have been in operation for less than 15 years,
the low quality of the impregnation process (presumably insufficiency of
seasoning, applied pressure etc.) has not provided the expected protection
against termites over a longer period of years.   Most of the poles and a



- 17 -
substantial number of cross arms are in very poor condition. The condition of
these lines has made it necessary for ESCOM to carry out preventive maintenance
on the wooden poles and cross arms regularly every week-end. In 1990, over 85Z
of the almost 600 hours of the planned outages and about 902 of the 190 hours of
the forced outages in ESCOM'S transmission system (Annex 2.4) were accounted for
by these lines. The proposed Project provides for the replacement of the wooden
structures by steel structures (para 4.i1C). The planned outages will become
negligible upon the completion of the proposed reconstruction which should also
substantially reduce the forced outages of the region. Lilongwe's reliability
of supply will also improve when the ongoing construction of the second 132kV
line between Nkula and Lilongwe (para. 4.2 and 4.5) is completed.
2.33      The system locses of ESCOM were reasonable at under 10% up to about
1980 (Annex 2.4).   Since then, however, the losses have been growing at an
increasing rate and presently stand at the high figure of 18.7Z.  A loss
reduction study, undertaken by ESCOM with the assistance of ESMAP, has identified
metar reading errors at power plants and main substations accounting for about
32 of the losses.   Administrative measures are in progress to reduce the
contribution of non-technical losses. The transmission and distribution system
study under the on-going Energy I Project will identify reinforcement and
expansion needs with due regard to improving systen reliability and losses. The
additional 132kV line between Nkula and Lilongwe, due for commissioning by 1993,
is also expected to reduce the losses at transmission level. The Project also
provides materials and equipment for reconductoring, sectionalizing the
distribution network, improving power factors and increasing distribution
transformer capacity, all of which are expected to improve the system losses to
at least 12% by mid-1990s. The Project also provides for improvement of ESCOMWs
billing and collection system through the acquisition of computer software and
hardware (para. 4.11 C and 4.11 E) which will contribute to the reduction of non-
technical losses.
III. THE ELECTRICITY MARKET
Recent Demand Trends
3.1       Total electricity sales on the ESCOM interconnected system rose from
404 GWh in 1985 to 569 GWh in 1990, an average annual increase of 7.12 (table 3.1
below). The highest increase was in the General/Commercial consumer category,
followed by the low density residential category; the lowest was in the small
industry category. Over the same period system maximum demand rose from 64.3 MW
to 95.9 NW, an average annual increase of 8.3% (Annex 3.1). These growth rates
were achieved despite modest average GDP growth rates of 3.1% per annum over the
period.
3.2       The composition of demand over the 1985-90 period is shown in Table
3.1. In 1990, the small industries category of consutmers accounted for 36% of
demand, twelve large industries for 302, residential consumers for 18% and the
general/commercial category fer the remaining 16%. The load factor in 1990 was
a high 67%, reflecting the large share of industrial demand in the total.

3.3     Demand Prosections.  With the assistance of Kennedy and Donkin Power
Systems, ESCOM has developed a relatively sophisticated demand forecasting model
which was used to develop demand projections for the 1991-2005 -period.  The
forecast methodology combiness
(a)   individual projections for the consumption of ESCOM's 12 largest
industrial consumers, based on detailed diocussion with the
consumersi
(b)   projections of the numbers of high and low density residential
consumers and their anticipated average consumption based on recent
actual experience; and
(c)   projections of sales to small industries, commercial and general
consumers based on official sectoral output projections and observed
elasticities of electricity demand with respect to output.
gd)   The resulting demand projections are then adjusted to reflect the
impact of anticipated increases in real electricity prices over the
forecast period using estimated price elasticities. The sensitive
consumers to price changes are those in Low Density Domestic and the
General Tariff categories.   The proposed tariff increases (para
5.15) are, in real terms, below the tariff increases used in the
model for demand projections. Projected system losses are added to
the resulting consumption forecast to estimate required generation.
Using observed load duration curves and coincidence factors, maximum
demand forecasts are derived from the generation forecast.
3.4     The resulting Base Case demand forecast which wae reviewed and agreed
by the mission, is shown in Annex 3.1. This forecast projects that consumption
of electric energy will rise at an annual average rate of 7.4Z over the 1991-2000
period.  Reflecting the results of ESCOM's planned loss reduction measures,
required generation is forecast to rise slightly less rapidly by 7.3% per year.
Maximum demand is forecast to rise by an average of 7.4% annually.
3.5     The Base Case forecast does not include any major new loads that are not
either under construction or firmly planned. However, ESCOM has had enquiries
concerning possible supply from three prospective large clients, VIPCOR, David



- 19 -
Whitehead and Mohinji Irrigation.  If it was to materialize, this additional
demand would add substantially to electrici 7 consumption and hence to required
generation. Based on information supplied by these three potential clients as
to the anticipated timing and size of the possible additional loads, ESCOM has
prepared a High Scenario demand forecast, which is shown in Annex 3.2. On this
forecast, sales would rise on average by 8.7% annually between 1991 and 2000 and
generation by 8.5%. In view of the uncertainty of these potential new loads,
generation system expansion is planned to meet the requirements of the Base Case
forecast. The prospects of the potential new loads will be monitored closely.
Should they begin materializing during the early part of the Project's
implementation period, the timi-ag of the installation of the additional units of
Kapichira (para 3.6) will be accelerated to satisfy the resulting additional
demand. The Low Case forecast, given in Annex 3.3. results in average sales
increase of 7.32, generation increase of 7.2% and maximum demand increase of 6.7%
during the same period.
3.6     Generation and Capaclty Balances.  The dependable energy generation
capability of the existing hydro system is estimated at 860 GWh, increasing to
926 GWh by 1995 following the targeted commissioning of Tedzani III and to 1329
GWh by 1998 following the planned commissioning of Kapichira in 1997.  The
projected capacity and generation balance (Annex 3.4) indicates that the system
will be energy constrained during 1993 to 1997 for the base, the high and the low
case demand scenarios. There will be ample energy generation capability from
1998 to 2000 for the base and low case demand scenarios; energy deficiencies will
result for the high demand scenario.
3.7       The  corresponding  dependable  capacity  of  the  existing  system,
including the 15MW standby gas turbine, is 136 MW. increasing tot 156MW with the
commissioning of the 20MW fifth unit at Nkula B in 1992, 196MW with the
commissioning of Tedzani III in 1994, 221MW with one unit of Kapichira in 1997
and 246MW with the second unit in 1998. The system demand will increase from
134.5MW to 252MW for the base demand scenario, with corresponding figures of
134.5MW and 270MW for the high scenario, during the period 1991-2000. Assuming
the planting program is achievable, there will be ample capacity for the base
case demand scenario during the period 1992-2000. Load shedding will be needed
if the high case scenario materializes.  The difference in capacity balance
between the Base Case and the Low Case scenarios is 11.6MW in 1997, which is less
than half the proposed unit size of 25MW to be installed in Kapichira. This
would not justify considering delaying the installation of one of the two
Kapichira units to be installed in 1997 should the demand forecast follow the Low
Demand scenario.

IV. POWER IESTMENT PROGRAH AND PROJECT
The Investment Program of the Power Sector (IPPS)
4.1     The Government and ESCOM with the assistance of consultants, have
prioritized the least cost options for power supply up to the turn of the
century.   These are based on Malawi's indigenous hydroelectric resources.
Studies, under the auspices of SADCC, are underway to assess the technical and
economic viability of further power interconnection in the SADCC region.
Potential options for Malawi are interconnection with Zambia, which may not be
possible from about mid-nineties due to Zambia's existing commitments, and
Mozambique.  ESCOM and Electridade de Mocambique have started discussions which
will lay down the ground work for future interconnection, having regard to the
proposed interconnection from Cahora Bassa to Zimbabwe. Interconnection would
be given consideration as a possible option for meeting Malawi's power needs
beyond the year 2005 when additional capacity is likely to be needed provided
that the political situation in the region is stabilized and additional
generation capacity is installed in the region. The high cost of imported fuel,
the uncertainty of supply and the low quality of indigenous coal make thermal
options uneconomic (para 6.1).
4.2     In accordance with the priorities ctstablishad for the least cost
development program, ESCOM is implementing the following components: the
installation of the fifth and final 20MW unit at Nkula B expected to be
commissioned in 1992; construction of 2x25MW hydroelectric power plant as a third
and final phase of Tadzani Falls scheme, including associated transmission and
substations, planned for commissioning in 1994; generation rehabilitation of
Nkula A hydroelectric power plant; construction of a 250km 132kV line from Nkula
B hydroelectric power ple t to Lilongwe, including associated substations,
scheduled for commissioning in 1993; and reinforcement and expansion of the
transmission, sub-transmission and distribution systems, including rural
electrification.   The major planned investment is the construction of the
Kapichira hydroelectric scheme. Kapichira will be constructed in two phases.
The first phase (Phase I) comprises a power house, which will be designed and
constructed to accommodate Sx25MW generating units, of which 2 units are planned
for commissioning in 1997, together with a 58km 132kV transmission line and
associated substations at Kapichira, Tedzani III and Blantyre West.   The
installation of the three additional generating units with their intake gates and
a short penstock (except for its excavation included in Phase I) will form Phase
II of Kapichira, tentatively scheduled for commissioning by 1999/2000 under a new
financing arrangement to be identified by Government by about 1993/94.  (The
major part of the civil works, all of the transmission line and the major part
of substation facilities for Phase II are unavoidably included in Phase I).
Reinforcement and expansion of the transmission and distribution systems form an
important component of the IPPS. A rural electrification subcomponent includes
the possible construction of a 4MW hydroelectric power plant in the Northern
region (Karonga and Chitipa, presently diesel supply centers) and extension of
the subtransmission system to other rural centers of activity in Malawi.
4.3     The IPPS has been defined and costed during preappraisal. Agreement has
been reached with ESCOM on its composition, and the priority components were
selected for inclusion ia the proposed Power V Project.

Consultants have been employed, prequalification of contractors for the main
civil works has been carried out and bid documents for a number of contract
packages are being distributed with bid opening dates scheduled for the first

transmission and distribution system studies are nearing completion. Computers,
mainly for billing and accounting, have been procured and are being installed;
(ii) the civil works for the installation of the fifth 20EW unit at Nkula B, due
for commissioning in 1992, is nearing completion; (iii) survey works for the
construction of the 250km 132kV line are completed and design is in progress; and
