(iv) transmission reinforcement and distribution reinforcement and expansion
works including rural electrification, are also in progress.
The Proiect
4.8       Project Obiectives.   The objective of the proposed Project is to
assist the Government and ESCOM implement the priority power component of the
least cost energy strategy adopted by the Government. The specific objectives
are to: (i) meet the growing demand for power in a least cost manner through the
construction of the Kapichira hydroelectric scheme and the rehabilitation of the
hydro, standby gas and diesel power plants; (ii) improving the quality of power
supply through reinforcing the transmission and distribution system to minimize
outages and reduce losses; (iii) strengthening the capacity of the energy sector
institutions to plan, manage and operate energy sector facilities and
institutions; and (iv) assist in rationalizing sector-wide energy pricing policy.
4.9       Rationale for IDA's Involvement.  A more rational strategy for the
energy sector, including least cost power development, decreasing dependence on
imported oil and coal and arresting the depletion of indigenous fuelwood
resources, is a key component of the Government's structural adjustment program
which the Bank is supporting. This Project directly supports the first two major
components of the energy sector major strategy and will make a contribution to
the third. First, it will help implement a coherent and least cost program of
measures to expand the availability and improve the reliability of power supply.
This will facilitate continued industrial, commercial and agricultural expansion,
which is a key component of structural adjustment, and increased household access
to electricity, which will improve living standards. Second, while involving
substantial up-front capital investment, it will minimize the long term economic
cost of meeting comme#cial energy demand by substituting lower cost indigenous
electricity for highe* cost, imported and unreliable supplies of petroleum and
coal. Third, by expa; ing household access to electricity, it will facilitate
the substitution of a cleaner, more efficient, more versatile fuel for
environmentally-damaging and increasingly scarce woodfuels, particularly in urban
areas - the most concentrated source of woodfuel demand. IDA has helped finance
three power projects in the past (totalling US$85 million, 8% of total lending
operations) and is, through the Energy I Project (US$31.9 million for power,
US$14.8 million for wood energy), presently involved in financing the fourth
power project as well as in the continuation of assistance to the energy sector
through activities on wood energy, technical assistance and studies.   IDA's
involvement under Power V would focus on the implementation of a least cost power
development program which IDA assisted in preparing. IDA would also assist in
rationalizing sector-wide energy pricing policy and in strengthening energy
sector institutions both of which are continuation of activities initiated under
the on-going Energy I Project. Equally importantly, IDA's involvement would
assist aid flow to the power sector and its application in the highest priority
areas.
4.10      Project Preparation.  The Bank agreed in 1988 with the Government and
ESCON on the recommendations of a study which update ESCOM's least cost power
development program prepared for the Government by Kennedy and Donkin in



23 -
association with WLPU consultants2/.   The study proved conclusively that
Tedzani III (presently under implementation as a component of Energy I) followed
by Kapichira ia the least cost solution to meet Malawi's power generation
requirements up to the turn of the century. The Bank's preparation mission for
the proposed Powver V Project which visited Malawi in April 1991 reviewed with the
Government and ESCOM the followings (i) the demand forecast up to the year 2005;
(ii) the feasibility study of the Kapichira hydroelectric scheme (the major
component of Power V) and its updated cost; and (iii) the transmission,
distribution and institutional subcomponents of the Project. The conclusions of
the least cost study referred to above and the findings of the preparation
mission constitute the proposed Power V Project. The proposed Project has been
reviewed in detail with Government, ESCOM and cofinanciers at appraisal in
August/September 1991, including the estimated project costs, the implementation
schedule and the financial capability of ESCOM.
4.11         Project Description. A detailed description of the Project is given
in Annex 4.1 and is summarized below:
Power CoMonent
The main features of the power component of the Project are the following:
A. Kagigihirg Hydrgolectric Scheme
(1)   construction of:  (i) an access road from Chikwawa Village to
Kapichira construction camp site; and (ii) camp facilities at the
construction site, including provision of water supply and
electricity;
(2)   construction of (i) an 800m long earth fill (1lm high) and composite
earth-rock fill (26m) dam at Kapichira Falls creating a pondage of
about 200 ha (total storage 11 million m39 live storage 3.5 million
m3) for daily regulation, provided with gated spillway; (ii) about
450m long power canal and power intake; (iii) 2 steel penstocks,
each lOOm long, 4.9m in diameter; (iv) a power house to accommodate
5 generating units of which 2 will be installed under the Project;
(v) a tailrace; and (vi) a switchyard;
(3)   installation of two Francis turbines coupled to two 25MW generators
with associated electromechanical equipment; a power house 2rane;
(4)   switchgear and control equipment for Kapichira Switchyard, including
two approximately 30MVA 11/132kV step up transformers; extension of
substations at Tedzani and Blantyre West;
(5)   construction of about 58km of 132kV single circuit transmission line
from Kapichira Switchyard to Tedzani III. The existing 132kV Nkula
to Nchalo line will be broken at Kapichira and the two ends diverted
into Kapichira Switchyard;
.a/    Study to update ESCOM's Least Cost Development Program, Kennedy and Donkin in
aasociation with WLPU, April 1988.



- 24 -
(6) extension of the existing SCADA system to include KapiAhirap and
upgrading as necessary the existing generation control microcomputer
center at Nkula B and interface of the telemetry software with the
existing systems:
(7)   establishment of single channel PLC and telephone links to Kapichira
to connect it to the existing system; and
(8)   (i) engineering services for the design, supervision and management
of the hydroelectric scheme; (ii) provision for a panel of experts
as may be needed to review the design and provide advice on
technical issues during the construction phase of Kapichira.
B. oGeneration Rehabilitation
Improving the availability of the existing power plants through:
(i)    the reconditioning or replacement of: runners, guide vanes,
cooling systems, etc., and provision of essential spares for
Tedzani I; provision of runner and essential spares for the
Nkula B machines;
(ii)   the rehabilitation of the intake structures of the Nkula A,
Nkula B and Tedzani power stations. The intake structures are
presently the major source of shutdowns of the power stations;
and
(iii)  the acquisition of spares for the gas turbine and diesel power
plants.
C. Transmission Reinforcement
Improving the reliability of the transmission system to the Northern Region
through:
(i)    the acquisition of tubular steel poles and steel cross-arms to
replace the existing wood poles and wooden cross-arms on the
66kV (designed and constructed for 132kV) line from Salima to
Chinteche (265km) and the 132kV line from Salima to Lilongwe
(75km);
(ii)   the  installation  of  the  steel poles  and  cross-arms  by
contractors for the Salima to Chintheche portion and by
ESCOM's own work force for the Salima to Lilongwe portion;
(iii)  the construction of a control building and installation of a
control system at Lilongwe, to improve data collection and
storage, reduce outage times and facilitate operations.
D. Distribution Reinforcement
Improving the reliability of and contributing to the loss reduction on the
distribution system through:



_ 25 -
(i)    installation about 23MVA 66/33kV substation transformers at
Zomba (1OMVA), Chirimba (7.514VA) and Monkey Bay (5MVA), and
1O0VA of 66/llkV substation transformer at Limbe and 5MVA of
33/llkV at Ntcheu;
(ii)   reconductoring of the llkV and Low Voltage systems in the
Southern, Central and Northern regions;
(iii)  construction of 219km of 33kV, 230km of 11kV, 120km of low
voltage overhead lines and underground cables; installation of
105NVA of 33/0.4kV and 25OMVA of 11/0.4kV distribution
transformers; and provision of energy meters, autoreclosers
and sectionalizers; acquisition of about 320 vehicles for use
mainly in the distribution systems.
E. Institutional Strengthening of ESCOM
(i)    consultancy services fort (a) studies to determine the least
cost option for power supply after Kapichira; and (b)
provision for expert services to advise on issues of technical
nature;
(ii)   establishing a strategy for computerizing ESCOM's financial
and technical operations, provision for the acquisition of one
central computer system at ESCOM's headquarters and 18
microcomputers (one for each district of ESCOM's area of
operations) to be linked through Lilongwe and Mzuzu to the
headquarters; and
(iii)  provision for training for ESCOM's staff, including
provision for training aids, to assist in building capacity to
plan, manage and operate ESCOM's facilities and systems.
Non-Power Components
The non-power components of the Project consist of the following:
F. Energv Planning and Policy
(i)    a household energy strategy study which will: establish a firm
data base for households energy supply and demand, examine the
supply and marketing of various forms of household energy,
with   emphasis   on   biomass,   including   substitution
posaibilities, and recommend a household energy strategy;
(ii)   provision of about 15 man months of expert services on an as-
needed basis, to assist the Government on energy related
issues including advice on petroleum or coal exploration (15
man months), and to address energy conservation and demand
management measures.



- 26 -
G- Petroleum Supnlv
Expert services of about 10 man months to review current petroleum product
procurement, transport and bulk storage arrangements and the legal framework of
the Petroleum Control Commission (PCC). The objective would be to idAntify
actions that the PCC could take to reduce the cost of petroleum product supply,
such as direct procurement and the construction and operation of bulk storage
facilities, and the changes needed in its legal framework to take those actions.
B. Energv Efficiency
Technical assistance to the Tobacco Control Commission totalling about 20
man months to demonstrate more energy efficient barn designs for tobacco curing
for smallholder farmers and to promote the use of softwood charcoal as a
substitute to the scarse hardwood charcoal for tobacco curing. Purchase of 4
wheel-drive vehicles and computer equipment. The main objective of this small
subcomponent is to serve as a long term catalyst in moderating the excessive
negative environmental impact of inefficient and scarse woodfuel resources.

4.13      The  total  cost  of  the  Project  including  physical  and  price
contingencies, but excluding duties and taxes (except for vehicles and workshop)
and interest during construction, is estimated at US$231.3 million of which the
foreign exchange cost would be US$167.7 million (72.52). The total financing
requirement including interest during construction is estimated at US$271.8
million. The base cost for Kapichira hydroelectric scheme has been updated, and
is at March 1990 price level.   The corresponding base costs for the other
subcomponents are based on 1990 procurements made by ESCOM and on information
furnished by the Government. Physical contingencies are 15Z for civil works and
10%  for electromechanical  equipment.   Price  contingencies were  calculated
assuming domestic inflation rates of 11.6% in 1990, 9% in 1991, and 6% in 1992
and 5% in 1993 through 1999. The corresponding international rates are 5.9% in
1990, 5% in 1991, 1.8% in 1992 and 1993, and 4.2% in 1994 through 1999.

4.15      The total foreign exchange requirement of the Project is estimated at
US$167.7 million of which US$166.4 million will be spent by ESCOM on the power
component of the Project, and US$1.25 million by the Government on non-power
energy related components. Of the total foreign exchange cost IDA is considering
to finance US$55 million, the African Development Bank US$27.7 million for
committment from February 1993, CDC US$25 million equivalent (Pounds sterling 15
million) and BIB US$18 million equivalent (ECUIS million).  There is thus a
financing gap of US$41.6 million (excluding US$0.4 million for training ESCOM's
staff abroads to be financed by ESCOM) for the foreign exchange costs which
require to be identified.   CDC has shown interest to finance, under a bilateral



- 28 -
arrangement, the transmission reinforcement component, estimated to require
US$16.3 million in foreign exchange, at a later stage of the project cycle. The
NORAD and NORDIC Development Fund are showing interest in the Project as a wholeo
particularly in the distribution reinforcement component, estimated at US$23.1
million in foreign exchange. In view of these indications, the appraisal mission
has agreed with the Government and ESCOM to leave transmission and distribution
components in the Project rather than transferring to the IPPS for implementation
as and when financing is available. ESCOM will be able to generate from its
operations the local currency requirements estimated at MK194 million (US$63.6
million equivalent) as well as the interest during construction estimated at
MK125.07 million (US$40.47 million). The Government is expected to finance the
local currency requirements, estimated at MK170000 (US$909000 equivalent), for
the non-power components of the Project.
4.16     Agreement was reached at negotiations that:  (i) the Government would
onlend to ESCOM US$53.75 million at 7.23% interest rate and 20 years repayment
period including a grace period of 5 years, ESCOM taking the foreign exchange
risk; and (ii) the execution of the onlending agreement would be a condition of
effectiveness of the IDA credit. The remaining US$1.25 million will be passed
on as a grant by the Government to: (i) the Department of Economic Planning and
Development (US$1.00 million equivalent); and (ii) the Petroleum Control
Commission (US$250,000 equivalent).
4.17      The notifications by CDC and EIB that all conditions precedent to the
first disbursement of their respective loans have been fulfilled would be a
condition of effectiveness of the proposed credit. During negotiations it was
agreed that the Government and ESCOM would conclude: (i) by not later than
February 28, 1993 an agreement with ADB for the financing of the two turbines
and generators needed for Phase I of Rapichira; and (ii) by not later than June
30, 1993, agreements for the financing of the transmission and distribution
reinforcement sub-components of the Project.
4.18     Project Imilementation.  Consultants will be employed initially under
a Project Preparation Facility, by about April, 1992.   Preparation of bid
documents and their distribution will be undertaken by the latter half of 1992,
and contract awards by early - mid 1993, rendering possible commencement of
construction immediately thereafter. Commissioning of Kapichira is targetted for
mid - 1997. Construction will be undertaken by contractors under the supervision
of consulting engineers and ESCOM. The rehabilitation of the hydro, the gas
turbine and the diesel plants will be undertaken by manufacturers under the
supervision of ESCOM assisted as required by consultants. The supervision of the
transmission and distribution works will be undertaken by ESCOM's own staff. The
transmission reinforcement work between Salima and Chinteche will be carried out
by a contractor. The portion between Salima and Lilongwe will be done by ESCOM
(para. 4.11C). A Project Implementation Schedule is given in Annex 4.4.
4.19     ESCOM is the responsible implementing agency for the power component
of the Project. ESCOM will appoint a manager for this component within the
Project Management Department of the existing organization structure (Annex 2.2)
who will have the following main responsibilities:   (i) co-ordinating the
supervision of the execution of the power component of the Project; (ii) liaising
with IDA, CDC, the designated body of the Republic of Austria and EIB on the sub-
components of the Project; (iii) dealing with all correspondence, procurement

employed for the engineering design and supervision of Kapichira hydroelectric
scheme.   The qualifications  and experience of the Project Manager  shall be

condition of disbursement of the Project.

procurement which will be subject to IDA's review is estimated at 72Z for the
Project.
4.21     Considerable delays have been experienced in the processing of ESCOM's
bid and contract documents for the on-going Energy I Project.  These delays
originate from two factors. The first ie the unwritten rule of the Government
that all correspondence by ESCOM with the Bank be made through the Ministry of
Finance.   The second is due to the practice, recently legalised, that all
procurements in excess of K250,000 (US$100,000) for works and K150.000
(US$60,000) for services require the Government's approval. The approval process
goes through the whole procurement cycle from clearance of bid or TOR documents
to final contract document (these result in up to 7 inter-governmental
communications for a single contract). The supervision missions of the Bank have
not been successful in bringing about a change in the present practice of the
Government. The specific recommendation made was that the Government's approval
be a one time operation, either at the bid evaluation stage (when approval to
negotiate and, if successful. conclude a contract can be granted) or at the
contract award stage (where Government can review the evaluation and the final
draft contract). At negotiations agreement was reached that: (i) ESCOM would
correspond directly with IDA, with copies to the Government as appropriate, on
matters related to project implementation and performance. It was also agreed
that ESCOM will correspond directly with IDA on all stages of the preparation of
bid documents up to and including their distribution to prospective bidders as
well as the evaluation of the bids.  Prior to contract award, ESCOM's bid
evaluation, together with the lowest responsive bid and draft contract, will then
be transmitted to the Government for review.   Following this review, the
Government will transmit its comments to IDA, and after obtaining IDA's no
objection, will authorize ESCOM to negotiate and, if successful, to conclude a
contract with the successful bidder.  The threshold value for the mandatory
review by Government would be increased to the equivalent or US$300,000 for
procurements pertinent to the power component of the Project.



- 31 -
4.22     Prolect Prenaration I'acility CPPF). A PPF of US$1,500,000 equivalent
has been approved by IDA to assist the Government finance the initial phases of
the engineering services for the Kapichira hydroelectric scheme.
4.23     Disbureement.  The proceeds from the credit would be disbursed over a
period of about 65 months from September 1992 through March 1998. Disbursements
from the credit would be in accordance with the disbursement procedures of IDA
and would be made for 100l of foreign expenditures for all project components.
Simplified disbursement procedures on the basis of Statement of Expenditures
would be allowed for civil work contracts of less than US$300,000 and equipment
contracts of less than US$100,000. Aniex 4.5 gives the anticipated disbursements
from the proposed IDA credit and is based on the estimated annual project costs
(of Annex 4.3) and are in compliance with standard profiles for Bank loans and
IDA credits for power projects in Africa region. However, disbursements for the
on-going Energy I Project are behind the original disbursement schedule. This
is mainly due to the long delay in the declaration of effectiveness of the credit
(11 months from the date of Board approval) and delays in processing procurements
referred in paragraph 4.21 above. Experience on projects processed after Energy
I indicate hardly any delays in declaring effectiveness. Bid documents for all
contracts for the power component of Energy I have been distributed, and
contracts are likely to be concluded prior to mid-1992, and the power component
is expected to be commissioned with a probable slight delay of about 3 months.
Procurements, with delivery periods of less than 18 months, are being concluded
for the wood energy component. Hence almost 100% of the Energy I credit is
expected to be disbursed by the original closing date of the credit (December 31,
1994). Possible disbursement lag due to probable delays in procurements were
discussed during negotiations (para 4.21). The closing date for the proposed
credit would be June 30, 1998.
4.24    ftecial Accounts.   To expedite the execution of the Project the
Government would open Special Accounts at a Commercial Bank acceptable to IDA.
Special Account A for ESCOM would have an initial deposit of US$1,000,000.
Special Account B for non-power components will also have initial deposits of
US$250,000. Payments from these accounts shall be made for eligible expenditures
under the credit. IDA will replenish the Special Accounts as needed. It was
agreed at negotiations that EPD, PCC and TCC would submit audited accounts,
including those of the Statement of Expenditures, to IDA within nine months of
the end of the Government's fiscal year.
4.25     Environmental Aspects.  Kap.chira hydroelectric scheme is a Category
A project for Environmental Assessment (EA). Accordingly, the Government has
undertaken an EA with the assistance of consultants. Concerned departments of
the Government and ESCOM were involved, and extensive discuasions in which
interested groups including NGOs were represented have taken place in Malawi.
The Government's EA final reportV/, dated 22 November, 1991, is available for
review at ESCOM's headquarters in Blantyre, and a summary is ptesented in Annex
4.7. The Kapichir. dam will inundate an area of about 200 ha and create a
pondage of about 11 million cubic meters. The feasib1lity study has outlined the
socio-environmental impacts of Rapichira. An EA assessment has also been carried
2/   Kapichira Environmental Aesessment Malawi Power V Project, 22 November 1991.



- 32 -
out by a consultant on behalf of the Government. The dam will inundate less than
1r0 ha of the 78,000 ha Majete Game reserve and about 100 ha of the 10,000 ha of
Blantyre City Fuelwood Plantation. Disturbances will also be created due to
borrow pits. The dam, being a daily flow regulating dam, will not affect the
flow of the river downstream and is not expected to have serious impacts to
fishery rejources in downstream areas, including Elephant Marsh. Compensatory
measures for the wildlife conversion of the 100 ha Majete Game Reserve could
involve: (i) setting aside of an area of equal size or ecological value adjacent
to the Reserve or as part of another park; or (ii) compensation in kind to
benefit the management and conservation of wildlands. The EA recommendation for
an electric fence around the Game Reserve was discussed at appraisal. zSCOM and
the Government have agreed that the proposed expensive measure is not warranted
in view of the negligibly small area of the wildlife conversion. ESCOM will
substantially reduce the area earmarked for camp facilities, presently 6 square
kilometers.   ESCOM has agreed to: (i) upon project completion, provide - if
required - guard houses (up to 5 guards) to the National Parks and Wildlife
Department of the Government at the camp area to compensate for the lOOha
wildlife conversion; (ii) establish an environmental unit to follow up mitigation
and monitoring plans during construction and after completion, assisted as may
be required by specialized Government Departments (Department of Research &
Environmental Affairs, Parks & Wildlife Department, etc.).  Improved tourist
access to the Game Reserve , increased economic development for local communities
and the provision of improved social services for local populations are positive
environmental impacts which would result from the construction of the Kapichira
hydroelectric project.  During negotiations agreement was reached that ESCOM
would: (i) compensate the wildlife conversion (less than 100 ha) either in kind
as indicated above or as may be agreed between ESCOM and Parks and Wildlife
Department; and (ii) estatlish an in-house environmental unit which would be
responsible for implementing mitigation measures and preparing monitoring plans
during construction and after completion.
4.26     The route of the 58km 132kV transmission line, feeding Kapichira's
output to the grid through Tedzani's 132kV switchyard, will follow closely an
existing 66kV line, thus reducing wayleave requirements. An EA has demonstrated
that the environmental impact of the proposed route will be less than those of
two shorter alternative routes. ESCOM will consult with and fully compensate the
resettlement of the affected people, some of whom are presently farming along the
chosen corridor. ESCOM will allow cultivation along the corridor but no building
will be allowed in the right-of-way. ESCOM and the Forestry Department have
agreed to clearing of some trees along the  A-lute.  The environmental <unit
referred to in paragraph 4.25 will be responsible for implementing monitoring
plans and mitigating measures.   Most of the transmission and distribution
reinforcement subcomponents of the Project are on existing facilities and will
not therefore introduce new elements of environmental concern.
4.27     Rigarian Rights.      Shire is an international river flowing into
Mozambique. Consequently, the Government of Malawi has notified the Government
of Nozambique of the proposed Kapichira hydroelectric dam, indicating that the
dam is a daily regulating dam and its implementation will not affect the
interests of Mozambique, and requesting a positive response on the subject. The
appraisal mission has satisfied itself that the construction of the Kapichira dam
will not adversely affect the quantity or the quality of water flowing into



- 33 -
Mozambique. The Government of Mozambique has communicated to the Republic of
Malawi its no objection, in principle, to the construction of the Kapichira dam.
Mozambique has suggested and Malawi has agreed to discuss, at some future date,
the technical data and the environmental aspect of existing and planned
hydroelectric power plants on the Shire river with the objective of ensuring
water supply and irrigation needs.
4.28     Project Risks. The principal risk of the Project lies on the probable
inaccuracy of the power demand forecast which could be adversely affected by an
unexpected economic or political situation of the region. This could result in
the new generating capacity of Kapichira coming on line too early. Conversely,
demand growth higher than forecast will bring Rapichira too late. These risks are
minimized by the staged development of the Kapichira scheme, 2x25MI units
(included in this Project) for commissioning by 1997 and, depending on the demand
growth, the remaining 3x25MW units (not included in the Project) by 1999/2000.
The risk of low free water level of Lake Malawi (unlikely in the medium term)
resulting from probable consecutive dry years would be mitigated through the
least cost contingent plan of dredging the channel connecting Lake Malawi to the
Shire river. This would allow further access to the additional live storage
below the free water level of the Lake. The project runs the risk of delay if
the financing gap (para 4.15) cannot be closed as early as possible. There is
also the possibility of a financial risk in the event that project costs exceed
the estimates. The cost update of Kapichira, undertaken by a consultant, makes
ample provision in the form of lump-sums7 and contingencies for the civil works,
and  higher  than  average  of  quoted  suppliers'  prices  for  the    major
electromechanical equipment minimize this risk. Should costs increase for other
subcomponents, their scope could be reduced without a substantial effect on the
overall objectives of the project. The institutional risks which may in general
hamper project implementation will be minimized through conditionalities (paras
1.19, 2.13, 4.21) included in the Project.
4.29     Project Monitorina and Sunervision.  Records and reports necessary to
monitor the progress of the Project and the achievement of its objectives were
discussed during Project preparation and will be agreed upon with the Government
and ESCOM during negotiations. During the lifetime of the Project, a minimum of
two supervision missions per year will be made to monitor target dates for
Project implementation , as per Annex 4.4 as well as cost estimates and
compliance to legal covenants. Performance indicators, per Annexes 2.3 and 4.6
would also be closely monitored. The reporting requirements will be explained
in detail during project launching to be done soon after the declaration of
effectiveness of the Project. Annex 4.8 gives indicative targets for project
supervision.  ESCON would prepare a Project Completion Report for the power
component within six months of project completion.

5.7        Accounting and AuditinM.  Under Credit 1990-MA1, ESCOM is obliged to
take all measures neceseary to ensure that the level of accounts receivable from

preceding twelve months. As can be seen from Table 5.3 above, the target has not
been met in the recent past. However, the deteriorating trend has now been
reversed and as a result of a tighter credit control policy adopted by ESCOM
since late 1990, they have succeeded in bringing down the ratio to 66 and 63 as
at March 31, 1991 and June 30, 1991 respectively. However, the receivables as
at March 31 (fiscal year end of both ESCOH and the Government) are always
expected to be higher since Government departments delay the settlement of their
accounts until after the year end following the new budget allocations. At
negotiations agreement was reached that ESCON would continue to keep ite accounts
receivable to no more than 60 days of sales.

5.9       Asset Revaluation and Degreciation.   ESB International Limited of
Dublin have completed the asset revaluation study4/ funded under Credit 1990-
MAI.   The results of the study and the asset valuation methodology were
considered and accepted by ESCOM.   However, ESCOM is of the view that the
financial statements should continue to be prepared under the modified historical
cost convention, whereby fixed assets are partially revalued to the extent of
exchange losses arising on long-term debt each year. ESCOM' s considered judgment
is that by incorporating fixed assets at replacement cost in the financial
statements would place ESCOM in an unfavorable light vis-a-vis other parastatals
and companies due to the significantly lower operating surplus and return on
assets which would be reported under the revised practice. According to ESCOM
the replacement cost method of accounting is not used by any company in Malawi
and is therefore concerned that the adoption by ESCOM of such a method will be
misunderstood and misinterpreted.   After discussions at appraisal during
September 1991, ESCOM has agreed to prepare as from 1991/92, supplementary
financial statements, incorporating fixed assets at replacement cost, which will
be presented together with the main historical cost financial statements and
reported upon by the external auditors  in their audit  report.    During
negotiations, agreement was reached that ESCOM would revalue its assets annually
and submit a supplementary financial statement based on revalued assets together
with its audited accounts.
5.10      The asset revaluation was carried out on the basis of replacement cost
of all the assets  in service.   Ueeful asset lives were determined  and
depreciation provided to give net asset values which would be depreciated over
the remaining asset lives.  The result of the revaluation was a three-fold
increase in net book value producing a revaluation surplus of Kwacha 480 million
approximately, compared to the historical equity of Kwacha 164 million at March
31, 1991. On replacemL... ist basis, the depreciation charge for 1990/91 would
have been Kwacha 35 million compared to the historic cost depreciation charge
(which equals the current Kwacha value of loan repayments in the year) of Kwacha
16 million.

board with effect from April 1, 1991 (previous increase of 10.0% on April 1,
1990). The average tariff for the current fiscal year 1991/92 is expected to be
t13.53 (USc4.67). Based on the present capital expenditure program, ESCOM is
expected to meet its financial targets of debt service coverage of 1.5 and a self
financing ratio of 30% for the current fiscal year 1991/92.

5.12      Under Credit 1990-MAI A tariff study was conducted under Credit 1990-
MAI by Price Waterhouse in association with British Electricity International
LtdS/.  A draft report was submitted to ESCOM in July 1991 and recommends
changes in the structure and adjustment in the rates of ESCOM's tariffs. The
draft study concludes that the rates in force are below the long run marginal
cost (LRMC) for the domestic and for the low voltage and medium voltage
industrial consumert, but are above the LRMC for the general tariff consumers.
In view of the substantial adjustments required (differences up to +36Z for the
former 3 categories and -222 for the latter), the study proposes a gradual
adjustment (over a 2 - 3 year period) of the existing rates to LRMC based -
tariff rates. The study also recommends the following changes in regard to the
tariff structure: (a) abolition of low density and high density domestic consumer
categories with a lifeline rate for the smallest domestic consumera; and (b) for
the industrial group of consumers, in lieu of the existing single category,
introduction of voltage - based maximum demand charges in two categories - low
voltage and medium voltage. It is recommended that the general tariff structure,
which embraces mainly commercial consumers and institutions (hospitals, schools
etc.), remains unchanged.   The appraisal mission discussed the consultant's
recommendations and IDA's comments with ESCOM.  The consultant is currently
reviewing ESCOM's and IDA's comments and a final report should be available prior
to negotiations. During negotiations, agreement was reached with the Goverrment
and ESCOM that IDA's view on the recommendations of the tariff study would be
taken into account and the recommendations so agreed shall be implemented over
a period not exceeding three years commencing not later than April 1, 1993. The
existing and the first draft of the proposed tariffs are shown in Annexes 5.7 and
5.8.

rate of return on revalued asset basis (para. 5.16) is almost half of an

result in much higher tariff increases than those indicated in paragraph 5.15
above6/.  Such increases are not warranted by ESCOM's financial requirements,
and will not be politically acceptable. Consequently# a self financing ratio is
included in the formulation of ESCOM's financial covenants in the proposed Power
V Project. Agreement was reached at negotiations that ESCOM would: (i) generate
from internal sources not less than 30% of the average annual capital expenditure
incurred or expected to be incurred over a three period, comprising the current
year, the years preceding and following the said current year; (ii) review
annually with IDA and the Government whether ESCOM will meet the requirements set
in (i); and (iii) if the requirements set out will not be met, together with the
Government take all necessary measures including adjustment to tariffa to comply
with the requirements.

VI ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION
Least cost Power System Expansion
6.1      The  alternatives  for meeting  Malawi's  forecast  requirement  for
additional power supply in 1997 are a new hydroelectric plant on the Shire river;
a coal fired thermal power plant; a gas turbine or diesel power plant; and
imports from neighboring Mozambique or Zambia. Imported fuel (coal and diesel
fuel) for power generation are high cost and unreliable (para. 1.2C).   The
average cost of energy from a coal fired power plant (unit sizes 25MW) is
estimated at almost 8 cents per kWh at 1990 price levels. The cost of fuel alone
for a 20MW gas turbine is estimated at almost 30 cents per kWh. The average cost
for a diesel power plant, with unit sizes of 15MW, is assessed at 13 cents per
kWh. The corresponding annuitized cost of energy generated from Kapichira, the
least cost hydro source of supply (Annex 6.1), is 2.8 cents per kWh. Neither
Mozambique nor Zambia will have significant quantities of surplus power available
for export to Malawi after the year 2000, which rules out the import option. The
Kapichira scheme is thus very clearly the least-cost source of additional supply.
Relationship of the Project to the Bank's Enerav Sector Objectives
6.2      In the electric power sub-sector, the Bank's primary objectives are to
promote least-cost expansion of the power system to serve viable new loads and
to optimize the reliability and reduce the cost of electricity supply.  The
project will support the first of these objectives by financing both the
Kapichira hydro-electric scheme, which is the next least-cost major source of
additional power supply, and selective reinforcement and extension of the power
distribution Gystem. It will support the second pair of objectives by financing



- 41 -
rehabilitation of the exiGting hydro generation facilities, replacement of the
unreliable Lilongwe-Chintheche transmission line and the uprating of overloaded
distribution transformers to improve system reliability and reduce technical
losses at the distribution level. In the petroleum sub-sector, the project will
support the Bank's objective of reducing the cost of petroleum supply by helping
the PCC identify and exploit lower-cost procurement options. The assistance in
energy planning will strengthen Government institutions and capability in this
area.
Economic Justification for Investment in new Sunnlv and Generation Rehabilitation
6.3      Assuming electricity demand expands in accordance with the Base case
forecast (para. 3.4 and Annex 3.1) the hydro system's firm energy capability will
be about 50 GWh less than projected energy demand in 1995, 125 GWh less than
demand in 1996 and almost 200 GWh less than demand in 1997   (Annex 3.3).
Available supplementary thermal generation capacity on the integrated system is
less than 20 MW, with an annual energy capability of about 85 GWh at a 50% load
factor. Should the 1995-97 period be characterized by low rainfall and river
flows, the power system would therefore be severely energy constrained,
particularly in the latter two years, even with maximum thermal generation. The
risk of this eventuality, and consequent load shedding and its high economic
cost, justifies both the commissioning of a new source of supply by 1997 at the
latest and the very modest planned expenditure on rehabilitation of the existing
hydro and thermal generating capacity to ensure its maximum availability during
the critical 1995-97 period.
Justification for the Transmission Line CoMRonent
6.4      The proposed new 132 kV Lilongwe - Chintheche transmission line will
replace an existing 132 kV wood pole line. The existing line, which currently
carries a load of 9 MW, is the sole means of bulk power supply to the northern
region of Malawi. It serves a number of sugar, tobacco, tea and coffee estates,
a plywood factory and several thousand residential consumers. Demand is growing
at over 7% per annum and could be supplemented by proposed paper, cement and
irrigation projects in the next few years. Constructed in 1980, the existing
wood pole line hao suffered from severe termite damage. It is currently shut
down for one day every two weeks for planned pole replacement and additionally
for an average of 120 hours per year by unplanned outages, which are increasing
by 5-10% per year. Replacement of the line with a 132 kV steel tower line is the
most cost-effective option to ensure reliable power supply. Using actual costs
of diesel generation on the isolated northern system (K 0.9/kWh) as a
conservative measure of outage cost to consumers, and factoring in the savings
in line repair costs, the economic rate of return of the transmission line
replacement is 12% (Annex 6e2).
Justification for the Distribution Reinforcement Component
6.5      The primary objectives of the project's distribution reinforcement
component are to reduce the current high rate of losses on the power system and
to satisfy growing demand for power. Transmission and distribution losses on the
interconnected system were 18.7% of units generated and 22.3% of maximum demand
in 1989, about twice the levels typical of an efficient power system. A loss
reduction study was undertaken in 1990 to identify the major sources of losses



- 42 -
in the distribution system, the inadequate capacity of which is the main cause
of the high system losses. This study identified substations and lines that are
overloaded, estimated the extent of the resulting losses and identified the least
cost option for reducing them. These costs were then compared with the value of
the losses avoided, estimated from the LRMC of power and energy, respectively
K407.3/kW and KO.04/kWh.  Sub-projects with an economic rate of return of over
15% were then selected for inclusion in the distribution reinforcement component.
Examples of the analysis performed are given in Annex 6.3 for one of the proposed
sub-projects  at  Thyolo.    Following  implementation  of  the  distribution
reinforcement program, it is estimated that losses will be reduced to 132 of
units generated.  This 5% reduction has been factored in to ESCOM's generation
planning and has thereby postponed the need for new generation capacity.
Economic Rate of Return
6.6      The estimated economic rate of return (ERR) of the project has been
calculated for the power system investments and technical assistance components
of the project combined, which represent over 981 of its total costs. The costs
and benefits of the later Phase It of the Kapichira scheme (Units 3, 4 and 5)
have been included in the analysis as these cannot logically be separated from
those of Phase I of the scheme. The ERR is defined as the discount rate at which
the present value of the capital, operating and maintenance costs of the power
components equals the present value of their forecast economic benefits. All
costs and benefits are expressed in 1990 constant prices. Incremental operating
and maintenance costs are estimated at 2% of capital costs, which is typical of
a  hydro-based  power  system.    Incremen_.al  benefits  for  the  generation
rehabilitation and transmission/distribution reinforcement components were
estimated separately from those of the Kapichira hydro-electric scheme, as
summarized in Annex 6.4.  The benefits of the generation rehabilitation and
transmission/distribution components were valued at the avoided cost of diesel
generation; the benefits of the Kapichira scheme (very conservatively) at the
planned 1998 tariff (the first year of service), deflated to a 1990 price
equivalent. Based on the above assumptions, the project's estimated economic
rate of return is 16.1Z, which is well above the estimated opportunity cost of
capital in Malawi.
6.7      The ERR was tested for sensitivity to the project's major riskse which
are judged to be underestimation of the capital cost of the Kapichira
hydroelectric scheme and overestimation of future demand growth. A 202 increase
in the capital cost of Kapichira would lower the ERR to 14.0Z, 202 slower growth
in demand than forecast would reduce it to 12.1%. The project therefore appears
economically Justified in the face of either risk.



- 43 -
VII. AGREELENTS REACHED AnD RECOMMENDATION
7.1      During neWotiations it was agreed that:
(a)   Government and ESCOM would consult on qualification and experience
of appointees to the posts of ESCON's General Manager and senior
positions reporting to the General Manager, and would take IDA's
view into consideration on the appointments (2.13).
(b)   Government would  take  IDA's view  into account prior  to the
implementation of the recommendations of the energy pricing study
and the recommendations so agreed to be put into effect commencing
from April 1, 1993 (2.23).
(c)   ESCOM would not undertake any major expansions in excess of US$5
million not included in the IPPS agreed with IDA unless ESCOM shall
have furnished to the Association satisefactory evidence that such an
investment is technically and economically Justified; (ii) the
Government and ESCOM shall review the IPPS with IDA annually and
agree on any changes (4.6).
(d)   Government would either conclude or cause ESCOM to conclude: (i) by
not later than February 28,1993 an agreement with AfDB for the
financing of the two turbines and generators needed for Phase I of
Kapichira (para 4.17); and (ii) by not later than June 30,1993,
agreements for the financing of the transmission and distribution
reinforcement sub-components of the Project.
(e)   (i)  ESCOM would be allowed to correspond directly with IDA on all
matters related to project implementation and performance; (ii)
Government's mandatory approval of procurements would be at the
contract award stage; and (iii) the threshold value for the
mandatory review by Government would be increased to US$3009000 for
procurements pertinent to the power component of the Project (for
detaile refer to para 4.21).
(f)   ESCOM would:  (i) compensate the wildlife conversion either in kind
or as may be agreed between ESCOM and the Department of Parks and
Wildlife; and (ii) establish an in-house environmental unit which
would be responsible for implementing environmental mitigation and
monitory plans (4.24); and (iii) compensate the resettlement of the
affected people along the route of the transmission line (4.25).
(g)   EPD, PCC and TCC would submit to IDA the audited accounts for the
non-power subcomponents, including the Statement of Expenditures
together with the Auditor's Reports Bank within nine months of the
end of each fiscal year (4.24).
(h)   ESCOM would continue to keep its accounts receivables to no more
than 60 days of sales (5.7).



- 44 -
(1)   ESCOM would have its accounts audited, including the Special Account
and the Statement of Expenditures, by independent auditors
acceptable to the Bank and sent to the Bank within four months after
ESCOM's fiscal year ends (5.8).
(j)   ESCOM would revalue its assets annually and submit a supplementary
financial statement based on revalued assets together with its
audited accounts (5.9);
(k)   IDA's view would be taken into account on the recommendations of the
tariff study and the recommendations so agreed shall be implemented
over a period not exceeding 3 years commencing not later than April
1, 1993 (5.12).
(1)   ESCOM would not incur any debt unless a reasonable forecast of its
revenue and expenditures show that the projected internal cash
generation is at least 1.5 times the projected debt service
requirement on all debt, including the debt to be incurred (5.16).
Cm)   (i) ESCOM would finance yearly from internal sources not loes  than
30% of its average capital expenditures incurred or expected to be
incurred over a three year period, comprising the current year, the
years preceding and following the said current year; (ii) review
annually with the Government and IDA whether ESCOM would meet the
requirement set out in (i)l and (iii) together with the Government
take all necessary measures including adjustments to tariffs (5.17).
7.2      Conditions of Effectiveness
(a)  Government would execute an on-lending agreement with ESCOM for
US$53.75 million at 7.23% interest rate and 20 years repayment
including 5 years grace (4.16).
(b)   Government and ESCOM would take appropriate actions to ensure that
notifications by CDC and BIB are received by IDA prior to the first
disbursement of their respective loans (4.17).
7.3      Conditlions of Disbursement
(a)   For the energy planning and policy subcomponent, placement of at
least one additional experienced economists in the Energy Division
of EPD (1.19).
(b)   For the power component, placement of a manager for the power
component of the Project (4.19).
7.4      Recommendation
With the above agreements, the Project is suitable for an IDA credit
of SDR39.2 million (US$55 million equivalent) to the Republic of Malawi at
standard terms, with maturity of 40 years including 10 years grace.

MALAWI
POWER V PROJECT
Detailed Project Description
A. DescriRtion of Kapichira Hydroelectric Scheme
Location of the Pawer Scheme
1.    The Kapichira hydroelectric power plant is located in the Shire river and
is shown on IBRD Map No. 23398.   A summary description of the power scheme,
including its associated features, is given in Chapter IV.  A more detailed
outline of Kapichira and of the other features of Power V project is given
hereunder.
Background
2.    Malawi generates over 99% of its power needs from hydro power.  A total of
144MW has been developed in a cascade group of power plants on the Shire river
at Nkula and Tedzani in four phases since 1966. An additional 20MW is being
installed at the existing Nkula B power station and is expected to be
commissioned early in 1992.
3.    The further development of hydro power to meet the growing demand of
Malawi's interconnected system was analyzed during the period (1984 - 1988). The
river basins considered included Songwe, South Rukuru, Bua, Rua and Shire. Only
the Shire river projects  (namely Kholombidzo, Tedzani III, Mpatamanga and
Kapichira) were retained for detailed studies on the basis of hydrology and
preliminary layouts. ESCOM's Least Cost Power Development Plan, prepared for the
Government in 1987/88, determined Tedzani III followed by Kapichira to be the
least cost option to meet Malawi's power needs for the period 1994-2005.
4.    The ongoing Energy I project includes the third stage of Tedzani Falls and
forms the fourth phase of power development on the Shire river. Tedzani III, due
for commissioning in 1994, will increase the installed capacity on the Shire
river by 50MW to 214MW. The proposed Power V project includes the first phase
of the 5x25MW Kapichira hydroelectric scheme, with 2 units expected to be
commissioned by 1997 (Phase I of Kapichira), and the remaining three units (Phase
II of Kapichira, not included in Power V project) by 1999.   The installed
capacity on the Shire river will increase to 264MW by 1977 and to 339MW by 1999
(over 57% of the potential of the Shire river).
Topographv
5.    The selected site near Kapichira Falls enables the construction of a
barrage of 20-25 meters maximum height upstream of the falls, developing a head
of about 60 meters.   The most economical minimum controlled pool level is
determined to be elevation 144.6 and the maximum 147.0. The selected site is



- 61 -
ANNEX 4.1
Page 2 of 10
well suited for the construction of a low spillway on the left bank with minimum
concrete requirements. The favorable topography has led to the selection of the
left bank for all concrete works, spillway, water conductors and power house.
The layout and design of the spillwyay is such that no major sedimentation
problems are expected at Kapichira; however, the need exists to analyze the
possible impact of sedimentation (Annex 4.1, para 9).
GeoloRv
6.    The  geology  at  the  site  has  been  investigated  both  by  surface
reconnaissance and subsurface exploration. Foundation conditions revealed by the
subsurface investigations indicate that the overburden materials (ranging from
1 to 1.50 meters in thickness) are composed of three recent alluvial terrace
deposits and thick residual materials (soil and weathered rock). The bedrock
underlying the residual materials is metamorphic and of Precambrian to lower
Paleozoic origin. The sound rock which is expected to be encountered below the
residual materials is considered suitable to support the earth/rockfill dam or
concrete structures.
HydroloR.
7.    All power plants on the Shire river are provided with daily regulation
reservoirs and depend on the upstream Lake Malawi (the Lake) for their annual and
longer term regulation.   The lake free water level of the Lake has varied
considerably in the past, falling to almost zero between 1890 and 1910 and
increasing steadily since then. During the last few decades the Lake's outflow
to the Shire river has averaged 650 cubic meters per second (cumecs). Kamuzu
(Liwonde) barrage, constructed at the outlet of the Lake in 1965, regulates the
floy to a minimum of 175 cumecs. Analyses carried out recently (Update ESCOM's
Least Cost Development Study. April 1988, Kennedy & Donkin in association with
WLPU) indicate that in the event of a return to the very dry conditions
experienced in the early years of the century, there could be risks of major
energy deficits. Based on a computer simulation model replicating historical
records of the Lake, risks could occur at any time from the mid-1990s and last
for over 5 years with energy deficits of up to 90X of total demand. Measures
considered to safeguard against a recurrence of very low flows included
construction of a thermal plant to substitute the hydro capacity, interconnection
with neighboring countries, and dredging of the upper Shire channel. The likely
and least cost option, requiring also the minimum time to implement, ls
determined to be dredging, to be acted upon when the level of the Lake reaches
an already defined predetermined trigger level. A gauging station is In place
at Mangochi to facilitate this monitoring.
8.    Monthly and annual flows, minimum flows available for power generation,
extreme floods and flood frequencies have been analyzed by TAMS under Phase I
Study (November 1984).  Under Phase II Study (February 1986), TAhS developed
additional parameters, in particular flood magnitudes for higher frequency floodo
including those appropriate for diversion design. Recently obtained flood marks
enabled the delineation of tailwater levels for various discharges.   Wator
surface profiles along the river and hence water surface of interest to the



- 62 -
ANEX 4.1
Page 3 of 10
design  of various  structures  were  determined.    Aerial  photographs  and
photogrametric and ground survey& have been used in the evaluations by TANS.
TAMS has adopted the maximum probable flood of 8900 cumace as the spillway design
flood which has also been used to set the flood protection level for the power
house. The annual flood at the Kapichira site is estimated to be 1000 cumecs and
the 100 year flood at 3750 cumecs. The impact of irrigation requirements, under
consideration by Government, to be extracted upstream of Kapichira will be
assessed by the engineering services consultant.
Pondaae
9.   The Kapichira scheme will be a run-of-river plant with no seasonal storage
and pondage will be required only to satisfy the daily peak demand.  The daily
load demand, as assessed by TAMS from ESCOI's load duration curve currently
available, exceeded 90Z of the maximum capacity for approximately 8 to 10 hours.
(TAMS has Indicated that this could change in the future.   TAMS has also
indicated the need to analyze the effect of sedimentation on pondage.   The
engineering services consultant will review and optimize the conceptual design
of the project, para 18 below). The Kapichira reservoir has been designed to
allow the operation of all 5 units at full load for a continuous period of 10
hours. For a continuous flow of 170 cumecs, the volume of water available for
generation equals approximately 3.5 million cumecs with allowance for siltation.
For this flow the minimum and corresponding maximum pool levels which will
provide this volume are given in the table which follows, with the levele in
brackets giving the selected levelo.

be subject of further deliberation in connection with the optimization study on
the technical features of Rapichira (para 18).
K&gichira Scheme
12.   The main features of Kapichira hydroelectric scheme are an 800m long
earthfill (llm) and composite earth-rockfill (26m) dam, a gated spillway, canal
intake, power canal, power intake, steel penstock, power house, tailrace
canal,switchyard, an access road from Chikawa to Kapichira, camp (dwelling,
canteen, offices) facilities at Kapichira, transmission line to Tedzani
switchyard, and additional supervisory system control and data acquisition
(SCADA) provision and power line carrier system (PLC).   Main data for the
proposed power scheme are as follows:
13.   The Power House.  The Kapichira power house will be designed to house five
25MW vertical axis generating units, with two units to be installed by 1997, as
part of Phase I of Kapichira, and the remaining three units by '999 as part of
Phase II.   Three of the 5x25MW turbine generating units,  including their
encasement, three of the 5 intake gates, the second penstock (except for the
excavation, which should be done in Phase I), and one feeder and 3 generating
bays at Kapichira switchyard will constitute Phase II of Kapichira and are not
included in the Power V Project.
14.   The vertical axis turbine will be of the Francis type, and will be able to
deliver 25.7MW, corresponding to a generator output of 25MW under either of the
following conditions:
--    power pool at its minimum controlled level (144.6 m) and tailwater
level corresponding to the required flow from the five turbinee with
no spillway flow; or
-     power pool at its maximum controlled level (147.0 m) and tailwater
level corresponding to a total river flow of 900 cumecs.
15.   The turbine will thus be rated to deliver 25.7MW under a rated net head of
55.9m as dictated by the second severe condition, with a corresponding flow of
52 cumecs per unit. The design head of 59.6m was calculated with the turbine
operating under average head conditions, i.e. a river flow of 321 cumecs, power
pool at its maximum level (147.0m) and the turbines at their best efficiency
point. This latter corresponds to a discharge of 41 cumeces (about 80 percent
guide vane opening). At full guide vane opening, under the design head of 59.6m,
each turbine will provide 28.9MW. A speed of 250 rpm was therofore chosen giving
a specific speed under the stated conditions of 257 (Kw;m). The corresponding
plant sigma is estimated to be 0.188, which is in the non-prone cavitation zone,
according to the standards established by the Bureau of Reclamations (Engineering
Monograph No. 2, Selecting Hydraulic Reaction Turbines).
16.   The vertical axis eynchronous generators will be directly coupled to the
turbines  rotating at 250 rpm.   The turbine will be capable of 27.8MM.
Consequently the generators will be rated 27MW, 0.9 pf, 30MNA. In order to limit



- 64 -
ANNEX 4.1
Page 5 of 10
the speed during load rejections to 45% of the nominal speed while maintaining
a guide vane closing time of 7 secs and to limit the pressure to 30%, the
generator motor will be required to have an inertia of not less than 1280 tm2,
corresponding to an inertia constant of 3.66KWs/kVA.
17.   Transmission and Switch_ard.    Tedzani  III Scheme provides  for the
installation of 132kV and 66kV transmission lines and bays at Nkula B power
station and at Blantyre West Substation. Phase I of the Kapichira hydroelectric
scheme will provide the following:
Mi)      Rapichira Switchyard.  Comprising a 132kV double design with
both busbars constructed to accommodate the ultimate ten
circuit arrangement.   Switchbays will be equipped for two
generator circuits with the generator transformers in the
switchyard, three feeder bays with circuit breaker on load by
pass   facilities   and   one   bus         coupler.   Remote
control/synchronizing facilities will be provided at this
stage to cover the arrangenant for Phase II of Kapichira.
Equipment for electrical protection, metering, a.c/d.c.
auxiliary power suppliee, llkV cabling, earthling, lighting,
multicore cabling, etc. will also be included.
(ii)      Tedzani III Switchyard.  The 132kV double switclhyard will ba
extended by one feeder bay suitable to terminate the single
circuit line from Kapichira. The circuit will be fitted with
on-load by-pass facilities and control, protection ,
synchronizing, instrumentation and will match the existing
equipment.
(111)      Tranamission Linea.  Kapichira will be connected to Tedzani
III via a 58km, 132kV single circuit transmission line, steel
tower construction with AAAC 175 mm2 conductor. The existing
132kV Blantyre West - Nkula line will be broken at Rapichira
and the two ends diverted into the Kapichira Switchyard.
(iv)      SCADA Syatem Extension.  The existing system will be extended
to Include Kapichira with similar facilities to those at
exicting outetations. Since it is likely that the original
design of SCADA equipment will be outdated and will not be
available for the extension, it is proposed to use a new type
of outstation , upgrading as necessary the existing generation
control center microcomputer at Nkula and interface the
telemetry software to the existing systems.
(v)      PLC and Telephone SYstema.   Single channel links will be
established between Kapichira and Tedzani III. Kapiciiira and
Nehalo, and Kapichira - Blantyre West. Use would be made of
the existing equipment between Nchalo and Blantyre West.  A
new PAX telephone system will be installed at Kapichira.



- 65 -
ANNEX 4.1
Page 6 of 10
18.   Engineering Services.  The Rapichlra scheme includes engineering services
to plan and supervise the construction of the power scheme. An estimated 1200
man months of services would be required. The terms of reference calls for the
following main services:
Stage 1: (i) reviewing existing studies on the project and carrying out
investigations as needed covering the hydrology, geology, topography and
the layout of Kapichira with the objective of optimizing the conceptual
design of the hydro-electric scheme; and (ii) preparing a Project
Conception Report, summarizing findings and preparing basic layout and
design drawings; (iII) a review by a panel of experts of the Project
Conception Report and provision for advice on problems of technical nature
during construction.
Stage 2: (i) prequalifying contractors for the scheme; (ii) preparing
final design and bid documents.
Stage 3: (ii) evaluating tenders and assistUing in contract negotiatione;
(ii)    supervising  the  civil works;  the  manufacture,  testing  and
commissioning of plant and equipment.
B. Generation Rehabilitation
19.   Tedzani.  Tedzani Stage I machines have been in operation for 17 years and
Stage II for 14 years. The high sand/silt suspension in the Shire river has
caused erosion of runner liners. guide vane bushings and seal sleeves. This has
resulted in detachment of rubber seals with consequential damage on runner bladee
and runner skirts. There is also leakage in the main inlet valves. A major
rehabilitation work would thus be needed on Tedzani units which would require
successive shutdown of the units for a period totalling 9 months. In view of the
capacity shortage, the rehabilitation work will commence in 1994/95, soon after
commissioning of Tedzani III. Works to be carried out includes replacement of
rubber seals for machines I and II (Stage I) as well as replacement of guide
vanes, guide vane bushes, sleeves, teflon materials, liners etc.; for Stages I
and II replacement of the turbine and the governor oil heat exchanger systems for
all 4 machines. The turbine and generator bearings, the electronic governor and
excitation of both Stages I and II require spares. Tedzani'a Generation Control
Center also requires spares.
20.   Nkula. Nkula A power station has been in service for nearly 25 years, and
Nkula B for just over 10 years.  Nkula headpond is not provtded with coarse
screens. The floating islands of vegetation which resulted from the 1991 flood
completely blocked the Nkula A intake screens. The headpond water level had to
bo drawn down by about 5 motero reoulting in tho oovoro rootriction of tho output
of Nkula B power station (adjacent to Nkula A); thio restriction was aggravated
with the accumalated silt deposits immediately upstream of the barrage.   In
addition ESCOM had carried out testo on Nkula A station to assess, among others,
head loesee In the water conductors. The teots established that the losses were
excessivo. The reason for this was confirmed during the physical inspection of



- 66 -
ANNEX 4.1
Page 7 of 10
the water conductors in March 1991. which showed their deterioration through the
formation of a slime layer, damaged concrete surface, bacteriological corrosion
in the penstock as well as substantial siltation in the tunnel. Major works,
including major maintenance of the water conductors, would be carried out in the
Nkula intakes through provision of course screens at Nkula B and extension of
Nkula A intake structures. Expert services would be needed to assess: (i) the
current siltation situation and the problem of the trash flow at Nkula B, and
determine, through hydraulic modelling or otherwise, measures needed to minimize
the impact of siltation; (ii) design the extension of Nkula intake structures;
and (iii) evaluate the condition of the tunnel and penstocks and specify measures
for their solution.  The scope of work at Nkula stations vould also include
maintenance and remedial works as well as painting work for the radial gates and
trash rakes of Nkula B. The maintenance would involve replacing radial gate
seals, flexible couplings and radial gate position transmitter units.
21.   Generation Spares.  Essential spares will be needed for the main inlet
valves, cooling systems, generator and turbine bearings, bushes and seals and
other consumable spares which may be needed for the proper operation of the hydro
plants. Spare parts will also be needed for Lilongwe and Mzuzu standby dieel
power plants as well as for the gas turbine in Blantyre. The project makes a
contingent provision for procurement of spares and for technical auditing to
assess ESCOM's requirements.
C. Transmission Reinforcement
22.   The capital city of Lilongwe and the outer reaches of the Northern Region
are presently supplied from Salima substation via: (i) a 75km 132kV impregnated
wooden pole transmission line to Lilongwe; and (ii) a 265km 66kV impregnated
wooden pole transmission line to Chinteche designed and constructed for operation
at 132kV. The impregnated wooden poles are in poor condition due to termite
attack and are the main causes of interruptions of power supply to the whole
Northern Region (para 2.31). Changing the wooden poles and cross arms by steel
structures will substantially improve the reliability of power supply to the
capital city of Lilongwe and to other parts of the Northern Region. The Project
provides for the acquisition and installation of tubular steel poles and steel
cross arms as well conductors and disc insulators to construct a line parallel
to the existing and to gradually replace the existing line.
23.   The specific provisions are: (i) acquisition oft 2500 about 14.4 m long
tubular steel poles for straight line tension suspension structures (two poles
per structure) complete with cross arms. number plates and associated
accessories; 152 about 14.4m long poles for angle steel structures (up to 60
degrees) and cross arms; 262 about 13.9 m long   poles for straight line
suspension structures and cross arms; 12 vertical poles about 12.3 m length for
4 terminal structures; about 860km of Mulberry conductor and 560km galvanized
earth wirel  disc insulators for the initial 5-10km of the line (subsequent
requirements will be transferred from the existing line); and (ii) lnstallation
of the 265km Salima to Chinteche portion of the line by a contractorg
installation of the Salima to Lilongwe portion of the line by ESCOM's own force,
after recovery of the conductor material from the Chinteche portion of the line



- 67 -
ANNEX 4.1
Page 8 of 10
and after completion of the second 1321cV line to Lilongwe.
24.   The subtransmission and distribution systems in Lilongwe are growing with
the increase in demand. A control building will be constructed in Lilongwe and
equipment will be installed to: (i) minimize the duration and reduce the impact
of interruptions as well as improve system performance through the introduction
of remote control of essential ciruit breakers; and (ii) introduce data
collection, storage and retrieval system.
D. Distribution
25.   Distribution reinforcement will be undertaken in the northern, central and
southern regions of ESCOM, focussing on areas identified by the loss reduction
study.  The reinforcement of the distribution network in the Monkey Bay and
Chirimba areas is in progress under a Spanish assisted program. The proposed
provides for the increase in the 66/33kV substation capacities at Zomba (IOMVA),
Monkey Bay (5MVA) and Chirimba (10MVA); 10MVA of 66/llkV substation capacity at
Limbe. In addition, the Project provides for the following: (i) uprating two
33/llkV 6MVA substation capacity to 8.5MVA; installing 1.5MAr shunt capacitance
in Thylo's distribution system; (ii) installation of 105MVA 33/0.4kV and 250MVA
of 11/0.4kV distribution transformers; constructing a total of about 220 km of
33kV, 230km of 11kV, about 10km of 11kV underground cables and 120km of low
voltage overhead lines; (iii) reconductoring about 250 km of 11kV lines in Thylo,
Mulange and Blantyre; (iv) procurement of about 25 of 66kV and 70 of 11kV circuit
breakers; 30 autoreclosers of 33kV and 70 of 11kV, 110 sectionalisers of 11kV;
(v) purchase of about 40.000 kWh meters including up to 1,000 prepayment meters
(if trials of the first 50 becomes feasible); and (vi) purchase of about 320
heavy duty and service vehicles for use in the extension, maintenance and
operation of the distribution system.
E. ESCOM's Institutional Strengethenina
26.    ESCOM's institutional strengthening comprises: consultancy services,
provision of computer hardware and software, and training. Consultancy services
will be needed to determine the project to follow Rapichira. Previous studies
have identified Kholombidzo and Mpatamanga, both on the Shire river, as possible
sequences of development after Kapichira (Annex 6.1).   The studies require
upgrading. Other options like interconnection with neighboring countries need
reconsideration, particularly if the construction of major hydro-electric plants
in the region materialize.    The Project provides about 70 man months for
consultancy services to determine the next project, in accordance with terms of
reference to be agreed with IDA. Computerization of ESCOM's financial operation
is presently in progress with computers procured under Energy I Project. ESCOM
intends to expand ite computer system to cover initially the billing of its
outlying dietricte and also to gradually introduce a management information
system. The specific requirements as presently conceived by ESCOM are two
supervision computers to be inetalled at the regional offices of Lilongwe and
Mzuzu.   Kaeungu,  100 km from Lilongwe, Dedza  (90 km), Mchinji  (150 km),
Nkaotakota (200 km), Salima (100 km), and Ntcheu (150 km) will each be provided
with PCs and, using daily-up telephonee, will be linked to Lilongwe's regional



- 6)8 -
AMNEX 4.1
Page 9 of 10
supervision computer. In like manner PCs will be installed each at Mzimba, 50
km from Hzuzu, Karonga (221 km), Rumphi (80 km), Chitipa (450 km) and Chinteche
(50 km). and each will be connected to Mzuzu via a telephone line. Each of the
seven district centers in the southern region (Balaku, Nchalo, Thylo, Mulange,
Zomba, Liwonde and Mangotchi) will likewise be linked to ESCOM's headquarters in
Blantyre.  The viability of ESCOM's intention needs evaluation.  A strategy,
setting priorities and defining specific requirements and their timing, needs to
be developed to expand ESCOM's computer system, including a gradual introduction
of a management information system.  The Project provides:  (i) about 20 man
months for expert services to develop a strategy, in accordance with terms of
reference to be agreea upon with IDA; and (li) hardware and software to meet
ESCOM's initial priority requirements.  Training and manpower development is
spelt out in ESCOMs 5-year corporate plan, and aims at continuation of
strengthening ESCOM's capability to plan, manage and operate its facilities.
ESCOM's training program emphasizes the fields of accounting, planning,
management and specilized fields in electrical engineering. Some of the programs
like accounting are sponsored in local colleges.   ESCOM resorts to training
abroad, either for graduate studies or short term courses, for specializations.
The Project provides for training abroad (under ESCOM funding) of about 10 man
years for graduate studies in the areas of engineering, accounting and utility
management. Short term courses in power system protection, system planning and
management will also be provided. The Project's bid documents will include about
200 man months for training about 30 electromechanical power plant technicians
during the final phases of plant assembly, testing and erection at site. The
engineering consultant will also be expected to carry out on-the-job training in
project design, supervision and procurement.
Non-Power CoMnonents
27.    Rner=w Planning and Policy.  The main activity under energy planning
focusses on developing a household energy strategy. The Government, under the
co-ordination of the Department of Economic Planning and Development, intends to
carry out a country wide household energy supply and demand survey, and develop
a policy addressing the strategy to follow in the medium and in the long term.
The Project will address urban households in the major towns in Malawi and
develop a methodology to be applied in rural areas. The scope of the study will
include assigrients to: (i) establish a firm data base on energy supply and
demand by urban households; and (ii) examine existing mechanisms and channels for
the supply, dietribution and marketing of the various fuelwood and other forms
of energy in use by households; (iii) examine the dynamics of interfuel
substitution in urban households; (iv) assess the impact of disseminating energy
efficient stoves on the demand for softwood and hardwood charcoal at target
centers; and (v) evaluate the economic and financial viability of a range of
policy interventions Xhat the Government requires to alleviate energy supply and
demand imbalances for the household sector, and recommend appropriate strategies.
The study will also recommend a methodology to follow for rural energy survey.
The technical assiotance will provide for training local staff in developing a
household energy strategy.   The Project provides about 200 man months for
coneultancy services.   Vehicles and computer hardware and Software are also
included.  A provolson of about 15 man months is also made   to assist the



- 69 -
ANNEX 421
Page 10 of 10
Government on energy issues, including coal and petroleum exploration, energy
efficiency initiatives for small industries, conservation and demand management
measures. IDA will review terms of reference for any of the activities prior to
their initiation.
28.    etolSupnlv.  The objective of the petroleum supply study is to
determine the least cost supply options for Malawi. The study will review the
sources for petroleum supply and their mode of transport, arrangements for bulk
storage and the need for strategic reserve, possibilities for bulk purchase and
bulk sales. The supervisory role for these activities is presently vested on the
Petroleum Control Commission (PCC). PCC is not mandated to either sanction or
give effective directives or carry out any of the above activities. Direct bulk
procurement and bulk sales, construction and operation of bulk storage facilities
by PCC would encourage competition in petroleum product supply and reduce costs.
The changes needed in the legal framework of PCC will be studied. Provision of
about 10 man months for consultancy is made in the Project.
29. Energa Efficiency. The estate sector, responsible for commercial tobacco
plantations, is intensifying its effort in introducing efficient woodfuel saving
barn designs and using charcoal from the thinnings of the Viphya plantations.
In an effort to benefit from this energy efficieny initiative, the Project will
investigate the technical and economic viability of reducing fuel usage in flue
and dark fired tobacco curing by smallholder farmers through the use of.more
efficient communal barns.  The Project will finance the services of an energy
efficiency specialist and an instrumentation expert for two years (1993. 1994)
at a quoted price of US$300,000. Support will also be provided for electronic
instruments and small vehicles (estimated at US$100,000).
