MONITORING GUIDELINES AND PERFORMANCE INDICATORS
Guidelines for monitoring performances on project implementation
have been discussed with ESCOM and Government organs. These guidelines
include a series of target dates in the Implementation Schedule of the
Project (Annex 4.4). forecasts against actual results would be closely
monitored by IDA for, among others, the following:                         0
(a)   preparation of TOR, invitation and contract award for
consultants;
(b)   issue of bid documents, bid closing, contract award;
(c)   progress of construction and installation of equipment;
(d)   project cost estimates and financing;
(e)   electricity sales and number of consumers by categories;
(f)   electricity generation, hydro and thermal;
(g)   system losses (% of net generation);
(h)   installed capacity; hydro and therrmial;
'i)   firm capacity and generation capability;
(j)   periodic forecast for sales and generation;
(k)   transmission and distribution facilities;
(1)   planned and unplanned outages and their duration for power
plants; transmission and distribution systems;
(m)   total and specific revenue from electricity sales by
category;
(n)   number of days of receivables;
(o)   number of employees per consumer;
(p)   self-generated funds contributed to investment;
(q)   debt-equity ratio;
(r)   debt service coverage;
(s)   audited accounts and annual activity reports.

ANNEX 4.7
Page 1 of 9
NALAWI
POWER V PROJECT
KAPICHItRA ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT
SUMMARY
IntroducLtion
1.    Malawi derives an overwhelming bulk of its energy from biomass and
hydropower sources. In 1989, woodfuel accounted for approximately 94% of
the country's energy supply with petroleum (3.8%), electricity (1.6%) and
coal (1%) accounting for the remainder. It has been estimated that the
remaining 4 million hectares of forests are being depleted at nearly 3.5
percent per annum. The most severe deforestation is taking place in the
southern region where population densities are highest and have been
exacerbated by the influx of 800,000 refugees fleeing from Mozambique.
2.    Hydropower development is concentrated on the Shire River, the
natural outfall of Lake Malawi. Installed generating capacity on the river
is 144 megawatts (MW).   It has been estimated that the river has a
potential hydropower capacity of 500 MW.  This potential includes the
proposed generating facility at Kapichira Falls in Chikwawa District as a
part of the World Bank Power V Project. Kapichira Falls is designed as a
5 x 25 MW facility so that generation of 125 MW can be achieved in phased
increments.
3.    Electricity is supplied by the Electricity Supply Commission of
Malawi (ESCOM) including construction, operation and maintenance of all
generating plants in the country.   ESCOM will be responsible for the
constructicn and operation of the Kapichira Scheme.
Project Description
4.    Malawi began developing hydropower on the Shire River in 1966/67
with the installation of a 24 MW (3 x 8 MW) plant at Nkula Falls followed
by an additional 80 MW (4 x 20 MW) at the same site. In 1973, ESCOM began
a phased generation schedule of development of the Tedzani Falls station
with 2 x 10 MW units followed by a 1 x 10 MW unit in 1976 and the last 1
x 10 MW unit in 1977.
5.    A least cost generation study by TANS Engineering Consultants in
1985 analyzed four Shire River sites at Kholombidzo, Tedzani III,
Mpatamanga and Kapichira Falls. Kholombidzo and Kapichira were judged to
be equal environmentally by TAMS and the consultants recommended that
Kapichira be constructed first.
6.    Kapichira Falls generating facility would consist of installing five
25 MW units in two phases, initially two for commissioning by 1997
followed by the remaining three units about the turn of the century
(1999/2000). Kapichira would be a run-of-river plant with no seasonal
storage and with less than 3% flood control. An llm meter high dam would
inundate approximately 200 ha of unoccupied land.   During the EA, a



- 87 -
ANNEX 4.7t
Page 2 of 9
possible discrepancy was found in the survey of the proposed reservoir and
the area to be inundated may have been overestimated. The dam would be
constructed upstream of the falle and water would be diverted to a power
waterway system consisting of a open concrete channel and two 6.2 meter
diameter power tunnels to the powerhouse approximately 450 meters
downstream of the dam. The design creates a gross head of 61 meters for
power development. The spillway, consisting of seven 13 x 13 m radial
gates has a design capacity of 8,900 cubic meters per second (cumecs)
which would assist in maintaining the intake area free of sediment
deposits. Trash racks and booms would be provided to safeguard the intake
channel.
7.    The powerhouse with space for five 25 MW Francis turbines will be
located in a deep, open-cut excavation downstream of the falls. Water
discharges would be conveyed by a 22 meter-wide open tailrace channel to
the main river channel.
8.    The dam and the spillway structure will be constructed on a natural
rock shelf which will require little excavation and use of concrete. The
spillway capacity is sufficient to pass the maximum probable flood of
8,900 cumecs without infringing on the 2 meter freeboard above the normal
maximum power pool.
9.    In order to provide access to the east side of the river, a bridge
will be constructed over the river just downstream of the powerhouse site.
The bridge will be constructed at an elevation of 1.5 meters over the
estimated one hundred year flood.
10.   A  construction  camp will  be  built west  of  the  Shire River
approximately four kilometers south of the dam and it will border Majete
Game Reserve. Access will be from the south on a partially-realigned and
up-graded existinG road. In addition, two areas south of the camp will be
available to the general contractors for staff and workshops. This area
will require construction of a new accees road linking the contractors
camp with the construction area. The camp facility will consist of 22
family houses, 12 apartment-style residences, offices, swimming pool, two
tennis courts and one squash court. A sewage collection system, primary
sewage treatment system and a water treatment plant will service the
construction and camp areas. Potable water supply will be drawn from the
Shire River. The camp is expected to occupy an area of approximately 600
ha. After construction, the camp will be used by ESCOM to provide housing
for opera'ion and maintenance staff of the generating facility.
Proiect Area
11.   Rapichira Falls is located 40 km southeast of Blantyre, the largest
city in Malawi and headquarters for ESCOM. The project erea is on or very
close to the southern border of the 78,000 ha Majete Game Reserve on the
west bank of the Shire River and less than 100 ha of this reserve could be
lost in reservoir inundation. The lO,OOO ha Blantyre City Forest Fuelwood
Reserve is located on the east bank and up to 100 ha of this reserve could
be lost by inundation.   Owing to the protected areas involved,  no



- 88 -
ANNEX 4.7
Page 3 of 9
involuntary resettlement of human populations will be necessary.   At
present, access to the. project site is via a poorly maintained road
leading to the game reserve.
12.   The project area is within the great trough which forms the Shire
River Valley and the highland walls of the eastern and western sides. In
common with the Luangw& and Zambezi Rifts, the development of the Nyasa
(Malawi) Rift is a result of a long history of intermittent uplift and
erosion with intervening periods of peneplanation and sedimentation. The
area is poorly endowed with minerals of economic interest.   The area
experiences tectonic activity with earthquakes being recorded up to 6.8 on
the Richter  Scale.   Accordingly,  the dam  design has  taken likely
earthquake activity into account.
13.   The Shire Valley experiences a "wet" season from November to March
with a mean annual rainfall of 902 millimeters (mm) and a "dry" season
from June to October. Rainfall is variable sea6bnally and annually with
an annual range from 710 to 1,774 mm. Mean maximr.Lm temperature in the dry
months vary from 28 to 35 degrees C. The mear, annual evaporation rate
since 1977 at Chikwawa is 1,855 mm.
14.   Malawi has been recording hydrological data from the Shire River
since 1897 and currently has an extensive network of hydrological stations
in the basin. The flow of the river consists of two major components;
outflow from Lake Malawi and the flow from the drainage basin downstream
of the lake. Outflow from the lake is the dominant component providing
between 40 to 80% of the river flow even in the wet season. During the
dry season, lake outflow accounts for virtually all of the river flow.
Lake outflow shows historic variability as evidenced by nearly zero flows
during the period between 1917 and 1935. During the past few decades,
river flows have been very high. For instance, between 1975 and 1985,
average annual outflow from Lake Malawi has been 653 cumecs. Successful
operation of Kapichira requires an average flow of not less than 170
cumecs.
15.   Lake control measures have been in place since 1965 when the Kamazu
Barrage was completed near Liwonde close to the lake outfall. The barrage
is currently operated under an agreement between ESCOM and the Water
Department following operating curve rules suggested from several sources.
16.   Water quality data are scant and only recently collected.  Ri-ver
water is rich in dissolved nutrients with moderately high hardness and
alkalinity. Water Ph data show a slight alkalinity at 7.54. Dissolved
oxygen concentrations are high given the aeration provided by numerous
falls and rapids marking the river's transit between Lake Malawi and the
downstream marshes in extreme southern Malawi.   The disrolved oxygen
concentration drops quickly as the water passes through the 60,000 ha
ElephauL ;iarsh domnatream of Kapi chira probably due to decomposition of
organic material in the marsh.
17.   Although the water is clear as it leaves Lake Malawi, suspended
solids (primarily silt) increase quickly to the point of causing problems



- 89 -
ANNEX 4.7
Page 4 of 9
with the cooling water systems at the existing power generating stations.
Mea urements at Tedzani in January 1991, show a range of suspended solids
between 83 and 1,521 mg/l with most samples exceeding 200 mg/l.  Soil
erosion is a serious problem in the Shire River Basin. As a run-of-river
generating station, the silt is not expected to be a serious problem in
the Kapichira reservoir, but will pose similar problems to the generating
machinery as is in other upstreamn stations.
18.   Elephant Marsh is a highly productive fishery with an annual yield
reaching 5,000 tonsO in good years from small-scale and subsistence
fishing. Fish yields have declined in recent years and fishing effort has
also declined (fewer fishermen). Several variables have been suggested as
possible reasons for this decline. Water levels in the marsh have become
more stable which may have contributed to reduced spawning habitat. Water
hyacinth (Eichhornia crassipes) has recently invaded the marsh and has
quickly spread with floating mats up to 300 m in diameter to become a
serious nuisance to fishermen.  A large crocodile population has also
discouraged fishing as crocodile-human incidents increased in recent
years. The primitive fishing methods invo'ving hand cast nets and long
lines makes the fishermen quite vulnerable to crocodile attacks.
19.   With the proximity of the 78,000 ha Majete Game Reserve and the
10,000 ha Blantyre City Forest Fuelwood Reserve, wildlife is plentiful in
the project area.   The game reserve boasts the fifth largest elephant
population in Malawi with a relatively stable population of about 100
individuals. Recent surveys indicate that the population is declining,
perhaps as a result of poaching or habitat loss outside the game reserve.
The Shire River is one of the very few reliable water sources for the
elephants and they are known to use the area immediately adjacent to the
proposed dam site for feeding and watering. The reserve also provides
habitat and protection for Sable Antelope, Greater Kudu and waterbuck
along with other smaller herbivores. Malawi places high priority on the
preservatic,r of waterbuck populations. Over 300 bird species havE been
recorded in the area (mostly from nearby Lengwe National Park) and the
threatened Rock Pratincole (Glareola nuchalis) uses the rapids near the
falls as an incubating subs' trate.   Similar habitat is located both
upstream and downstream of the proposed site. Hippos occupy parts of the
river with the largest population found in Elephant Marsh. It is likely
that hippos would be quick to favor the more placid waters of the proposed
reservoir.
20.   Extensive botanical surveys of the project area have not been
conducted, but the riverine area includes a relatively intact gallery
forest in the reservoir zone as well as above and below the proposed
reservoir. Forest surveys are being conducted on the east side of the
river as a part of the fuelwood project. A survey of the trees in the
zone to be inundated was conducted as a part of the EA and it was
estimated that between 18,000 and 24,000 trees will be lost.
21.   The falls are a major zoogeographic barrier for fish, separating the
dowmstream lower Zambezi fish fauna from the Lake Malawi fish fauna.  This
barrier is important in maintaining a separation of the highly endemic



- 90 -
ANNEX 4.7
Page 5 of 9
species of Lake Malawi from the dow,nstream species.  The daraj would not
decrease the value of this barrier but would reinforce the separation.
22.   Human populations are dispersed between numerous small villages
along the present access road which is nearly impassable during the wet
season.   This area is densely populated and the population growth is
higher than the national population growth rate of 3.7% per annum. The
problem is worsened by an influx of nearly 800,000 refugees from
Mozambique to southern Malawi, many living close to the project area. A
large refugee camp on the northern border of Majete Game Reserve is
increasing pressure on the natural resources (fuelwood and wildlife) of
the reserve.
23.   During the environmental assessment (EA), meetings were held in
three villages close to the project site. The majority of the people at
the meetings were aware of the proposed project, but had never been
directly or systematically informed by ESCOM. A large majority expressed
beliefs of increased economic opportunities arising from the project,
mostly in the area of construction employment or increased business
opportunities. The people did not perceive benefits in improved housing
or access to markets.   The people also did not believe that access to
electricity would be a factor in project development.
24.   During the EA, local NGOs were invited to participate in meetings at
various levels. Most NGOs were obliged to limLt their participation owing
to a lack of funds unless the EA team could cover their expenses for
attending meetings or participating in field studies. The government was
able to provide a small amount for funds to cover NGO expenses at EA
meetings.   At least one NGO offered assistance to ESCOM to assist in
working with local populations during project implementation.
25.   Inadequate health care is a serious problem in the project area as
evidenced by an infant mo)rtality rate in Chikwawa District of 205 per
1,000 which is higher than the national average (151/1,000) and the
average  for Sub-Saharan Africa  of  106/1,000.    Admissions  data  for
hospitals in the Southern Region show the following reasons for admittance
(in decreasing order of frequency); malnutrition, diarrhea, anemia,
malaria, measles, pneumonia and tuberculosis. The prevalence of AIDS is
thought to be increasing with over an 18% HIV infection rate in one study
of blood donors screened between January and August 1989 in Chikwawa
District.
26.   Schistosomiasis and onchocerciasis are present in the project area,
but are not considered to be serious by health officials, at least not as
serious as other reported diseases.
27.   Within Chikwawa District, there are three hospitals, ten health
centers, three dispensaries, six clinics, 12 health posts, one maternity
center, one public health center and 42 outreach clinics to serve a
population of 314,000 people (1987 data).



- 91 -
ANNEX 4.7
Page 6 of 9
Probable Project Impacts
28.   Land loss will be limited to approximately 200 ha of 88,000 ha
protected wildlands for the reservoir and 600 ha of unoccupied land for a
construction yard and labor camp. No involuntary resettlement of human
populations will be required with the possible exception of some
relocation during access road realignment and construction of the
transmission line from Kapichira to Tedzani (para. 43).
29.   No significant health impacts are expected as the reservoir will not
create conducive habitat for mosquiros or bilharzia snails. Increased
economic development associated with dam construction may reduce the
economic factors contributing to malnutrition and water supplies will be
improved.   One exception could be a significant  increase  in AIDS.
Sexually-transmitted diseaaes are a rnormal factor in large construction
projects owing to higher wages and an influx of a younger, more sexually
active population. In past projects, contractor's clinics were able to
control venereal diseases with availability of health post staff and the
diseases could be treated with relatively inexpensive antibiotics. AIDS
will not respond to, nor be controlled with health care or medicines at
this time, thus it will require the contractor to aggressively pursue a
health education program for the labor force.
30.   Plant and 'animal life will be lost (200 ha) or disturbed.   The
elephant population will be unable to use the construction site for a
watering point as they do at present. This disruption will be temporary
and the elephants will be able to use protected upstream areas as an
alternative.   Following construction, it is expected that the wildlife
will return to the immediate project area and will benefit from the larger
aquatic area created by the reservoir.  The threatened Rock Pratincole
will lose nesting and feeding habitat immediately above the dam while
areas below the dam should not be affected.   Hippo and crocodile
populations are likely to benefit from the reservoir. Avifauna in general
will benefit from the creation of a 200 ha reservoir.
31.   Fish movements will not be altered,  nor should there be any
significant changes in the Elephant Marsh fishery.  To a small degree,
fishing will be improved in the reservoir due to improved fish habitat.
However, ESCOM should not permit the introduction of any exotic fish
species into the reservoir for any reason.
31.   Water Hyacinth is present in downstream areas and could eventually
become established in the reservoir. However, swift water movement and
frequent drawdowns are not conducive to the spread of this water weed. It
is not likely that waterweeds will pose an environmental threat to the
operation of the facility.
32.   The short retention time in this basically run-of-river reservoir
will not alter water quality.   Anaerobic decomposition will not be a
factor in reservoir operation. Powerhouse wastes, such as lubricants and



- 92 -             ANNEX 4.7
Page 7 of 9
solvents, will be controlled and will not be discharged into the tailrace
channel.
33.   While the project itself will not increase watershed erosion, there
is an existing erosion problem which could adversely affect the operation
of the generating machinery. The design of the dam will help to remove
sediments during heavy flows thereby reducing the adverse effects of
reservoir sedimentation.
34.   Downstream hydrology will only be slightly affected during dry
season flows. Elephant Marsh will still receive annual floods, but of a
slightly lower magnitude.  Dry season flows will be more stable which
might be slightly detrimental downstream fisheries. The presence of two
upstream hydropower stations indicates that run-of-river stations have
been operating on the Shire River without serious ill effects on
downstream fisheries.
35.   Improved  access  to Majete  Game  Reserve  may  increase  tourism
especially if some of the construction camp infrastructure can be retained
for tourism Aevelopment. Since the access road will end at the reserve
boundary, it is not expected to contribute to increased poaching.   If
tourism increa,zes, the net effect could be reduced poaching as a result of
an enlarged guard and ranger force.
36.   Borrow pits have been identified within the game reserve and these
will create additional disturbance to wildlife populations of a temporary
nature. With some precaution and eventual restoration, this disturbance
can be kept at an acceptable level.
37.   Given the employment opportunities during construction, there will
be an influx of laborers and secondary economic activities, such as shops,
restaurants and camp merchants. While providing local economic benefits,
these can be short-term and have deleterious side-effects, such as
increased alcohol consumption, sexually-transmitted diseases and local
crime. ESCOM has a good record of not allowing the adverse aspects of
construction "boom-town" conditions to become established.   There is
little evidence of adverse social effects at the former construction sites
at Nkula and Tedzani.
Recommended Mitigation Measures
38.   ESCOM can mitigate most of the adverse consequences of construction
by insisting on strict environmental protection clauses in the
contractor's construction contracts.  The company must insist that the
contractor respond quickly to any increased erosion caused by construction
activities rather than wait to restore conditions at the end of the
construction period. Access road erosion must be kept in check with sound
engineering design and implementation. If erosion happens to occur, the
contractor must fix it immediately or be subject to financial penalties.
39.   The contracts should have sufficient wildland protection clauses.
Employees should be subject to immediate dismissal for poaching or



93 -           ANNEX 4.7
Page 8 of 9
harassing wildlife or unduly damaging native vegetation, especially in the
protected areas. Borrow pits which represent threats to wildlife should
be fenced or patrolled by game reserve wardens. The borrow pits should be
restored to natural contours and revegetated with native vegetation as
soon as they are no longer in use. Access roads should also be protected
with natural vegetation and adequate drainage so as not to permit soil
movement into the Shire River.
40.   Since wildlands of special interest will be lost, the World Bank
Wildlands Policy requires compensatory actions for the loss. ESCOM will
need to meet with the Department of Wildlife and National Parks to agree
upon a suitable compensation.   This compensation need not be limitbd
solely to adding an equivalent amount of land to the Majete Game Reserve
or other national park, but could include institutional strengthening for
park protection or retaining some of the construction camp buildings as
game reserve infrastructure.
41.   The contractor should guarantee the health of all employees with
medical screening as a pre-requisite to employment including HIV
screening.    The  contractor  should  provide  health  services  for  all
employees and their families,  including the unskilled workers.   The
contractor should engage a local NGO or private company to conduct a
health education program including material regarding transmission of AIDS
for workers and their families.
42.   ESCOM has agreed to create an in-house environmental unit as soon as
possible.   This unit will begin with one social scientist and one
environmental scientist charged with the initial task of defining the
mitigation clauses for construction firm contracts. Later, the unit should
be expanded to include technician support to assist the professionals in
conducting  field  surveys  and  environmental  supervision.         During
construction, this unit should be housed at the construction site and
provided with laboratory and rield equipment necessary for periodic
monitoring of construction activities.   This unit should be the ESCOM
liaison point with affected populations, NGOs and government agencies,
such as the Department of Wildlife and National Parks.
43.   The Government has conducted an in-house EA of the 58 km 132 kV
transmission line associated with the Kapichira Project which has been
reviewed by tho Government's consultant. The route follows closely the
route of an existing transmission line.   Some  10 families will be
resettled. ESCOM will allow cultivation along the route and no farm land
will be lost.   ESCOM will consult with the affected people and will
compensate fully their resettlement.
44.   ESCOM,  in collaboration with the Forestry Department  and  the
Blantyre City Fuelwood Project, has made arrangements to insure that all
woody vegetation will be cut and removed from the future inundation zone.
Herbicides should not be used in the clearing program except for stump
treatment to prevent re-sprouting.



- 94 -
ANNEX 4.7
Page 9 of 9
45.   ESCOM, in collaboration with the Hydrology Department, should plan
and implement a water quality monitoring program at one of the hydropower
sites, preferably at Kapichira Falls as it will be the most downstream
facility in the generating network.   The Hydrology Department and the
Fisheries Department will continue to monitor flow in the Shire River and
water levels in Elephant Marsh.
46.   ESCOM, in collaboration with the Council of Social Welfare, should
plan and implement an inexpensive system of monitoring socioeconomic
changes in the villages rnearby the construction site.   To the extent
possible, ESCOM should give preference to local labor and local vendors
during the construction period.
O7.   ESCOM has agreed to support a botanical survey by the National
Herbarium and an archeological survey by the Department of Antiquities
within the proposed reservoir area.
48.   ESCOM  has  accepted  the  recommendation  that  no  uncontrolled
settlement and unregulated land use will take place within two kilometers
of the project area and camp site. Fuelwood cutting will only be allowed
in areas identified by the Forestry Department.
49.   ESCOM will fence the camp site and project area to limit wildlife
movement into living and working areas.
Interagency Neetin s
50.   During the EA field work, two interagency meetings were held under
the joint sponsorship of the Department of E^onomic Planning and
Development (EP&D) and National Environmental Secretariat.   The first
meeting, a scoping exercise, defined the issues to be studied and assigned
responsibilities and schedules for completing the necessary work.  The
second meeting, attended by NGOs, presented the results of the EA and
sought suggestions for mitigation actions to be taken. Several excellent
proposals came out of this meeting, but mostly directed at longer-term
government actions rather than focusing on actions that ESCOM could take
to reduce potential adverse effects of constructing and operating a
hydropower  station at Kapichira Falls.    These proposals  include  a
recommendation for establishing a Shire Basin Water Management Authority,
developing a natural resource management plan for the Shire Basin
watershed, a riverine vegetation protection plan and strengthening the
Environmental Secretariat of the Department of Research and Environmental
Affairs. These proposals have considerable merit and it is foreseen that
they will be seriously considered by government. It is clear, however,
that ESCOM, the implementing institution for the Kapichira Generating
Station should take a participatory role, not a leading role in the follow
up for the basin-wide planning recommendations which emerged from the
final EA meeting.

1.    The timing for supervision and the staff weeks indicated in this Annex are
tentative and will be adjusted in line with future developments.   Normal
headquarter activities on procurement, follow-up of project implementation and
other project related activities are not included in this Annex.   They are
estimated to require at least seven staff weeks during the first year, and six
ataff weeks per year during subsequent years up to project completion. The plan
does not also include missions in the event of abnormal developments during
construction.
Borrowers Contribution to Supervision
2.    Quarterly reports are to be submitted by ESCOM following project launching
until project completion. The format of the progress report will be agreed with
IDA at project launch.  Updating project costs, including cost estimates to
completion, and compiling relevant data on physical implementation will be
prepared by ESCOM prior to arrival of supervision missions, in addition to
specific requests that may be needed by visiting supervision missions.  The
Borrower will also be expected to provide a substantial input to the Project
Completion Report.

DEFINITION AND A WOR&ING EXAMPLE OF THE SELF FIYANOING RATIO
Definition
Self financing ratio is the ratio o.'
funds generated from operatiore (gross revenues minus
operating expenses excluding non cash items, such as
depreciation and contributions and grants released to
income, and interest) plus consumers' deposits and
contributions received; minus debt service; minus/plus
increase/decrease in workii',g capital other than cash, the
annual average of capital expenditure (including interest
during construction) for the current year, the previous year
and the following fiscal year.

HALAWI
POWER V PROJECT
ELECTRICITY SUPPLY COMMISSION OF MALAWI (ESCOM)
NOTES AND ASSUMPTIONS FOR THE FINANCIAL PROJECTIONS
Sales and Revenues
1.          Sales projections are based on ESCOM's base case scenario
given in Annex 3.1 and reviewed by the Bank. Sales by calendar year
have been time-apportioned to derive sales by fiscal year. Sales
revenues are based on yearly average tariff increases, to be introduced
at the beginning of each fiscal year, of 12% in 1992/93 through to
1994/95, and 7.5% in 1995/96 and 1998/99. No increases in tariff have
boen provided for in 1996/97 and 1997/98. For 1991/92, the actual
average tariff increase of 7.5% implemented on April 1, 1991 has been
applied. "Other" revenue is assumed to increase at an annual rate of
10% throughout.
Operatip_g Costs
2.          Salaries have been increased by 10% annually except for
1994/95 and 1996/97 when a 12% increase has been applied for each year.
Normal fuel consumption is escalated at 25% per annum to 1994/95 and 10%
thereafter. Increase in consumption is taken in 1992/93, the year prior
to the completion of Tedzani III hydro development. The diesel stations
at Karonga and Chitipa will be replaced by a 4MW hydro plant due to be
commissioned in 1995/96. Current fuel prices have been used and
adjusted for movements in exchange rates as forecast. Repairs and
maintenance cost is assumed to increase by 15% per annum from 1991/92.
Transport, and administration and supply costs are assumed to increase
each year in line with local inflation plus 5% for growth from 1991/92.
It has been assumed that no corporate taxes will arise through out due
to the unutilized tax losses at March 31, 1991 of K32 million and
further investment allowances available from the heavy investment
program planned for the future (para. 2.22)>
Depreciation
3.          New civil works for Tedzani III and Kapichira are
depreciated over 80 years after commissioning as per ESCOM's policy.
Depreciation on all other assets has been charged at 3% of the closing
revalued gross fixed assets in line with past historical average and the
rate derived on the basis of revalued assets for 1990/91.
Fixed Assets
4.          Fixed assets and accumulated depreciation have been stated
at replacement coAt on the basis of a professional valuation conducted
by ESB International Limited of Dublin. The valuation has had the
effect of increasing the historical gross value by 3.45 times and the



- 104 -
ANNEX 5.6
Page 2 of 3
historical accumulated deprecation by 4.44 times resulting in a three-
fold increase in net asset value as at April 1, 1991. Thereafter, the
asset values are adjusted to reflect yearly adjustments to the kwacha
(based on 75% foreign and 25% local components) to take into account the
difference between international and domestic inflation. The following
asumptions are made for international and domestic inflation:
Year ending March 31:   1992  1993  1994  1995  1996  1997  1998  1999
International Inflation 4.2   1.8   244   4.2   4.2   4.2   4.2   4.2
Domestic Inflation       8.2   5.7   5.0   5.0   5.0   5.0   5.0   5.0
Current Assets
S.          Accounts receivable have been estimated at 60 days's annual
sales; inventory is assumed to increase by KO.5 million each year;
prepayments and advances have been assumed to increase at 10% each year.
Current Liabilities
6.          Accounts payable are taken at 60 days of operating expenses,
excluding salaries, corporate taxes and depreciation. Project creditors
for local and foreign suppliers have been estimated at 30 and 60 days of
the local and foreign components of construction costs respectively.
Consumer deposits are set to increase by 15% per annum throughout the
forecast period. Consumer contributions in aid of capital expenditure
is assumed at 90% of the distribution expansion program; and 50% of the
Chintheche/Chikangawa 132 kV line and substation, in line with present
practice or anticipated contribution.
7.          It is assuiaed that the foreign currency component of all
proposed projects included in ESCOM's investment program will be funded
from soft-term loans except for: projects with identified grants; and
foreign currency requirements for the dia ribution expansion program as
from 1996/97. the Chinteche/Chickangawa 132 kV line and substation, and
ESCOM general which will be financed from internally generated funds.
8.          Financing for the Power V Project has been assumed as
follows after discussions with cofinanciers: IDA.US$48.75 million on-
lent to ESCOM on IBRD terms; CDC UKP15 million direct loan to ESCOM at
an interest rate of 8.5% per annum, being the interest rate on the
latest CDC loan to ESCOM; EIB's ECU14 million will be on-lent to ESCOM
in kwacha at IBRD interest rate and repayable over 18 years including 3
years' grace; and the financing gap has been assumed to be on-lent to
ESCOM on IBED terms.
9.          Funding for foreign currency requirements for all other
proposed projects has been assumed on IBRD terms except for rural
electrification projects for which an interest rate of 3% per annum has
been assumed in line with the maximum permissible rate under the present
government guidelines.

ANNEX 5.6
Page 3 of 3
10.         IBRD terms assumes on-lent loan from the government to ESCOM
at an interest rate of 7.65% per annum, repayable over 20 years
including 5 years will be capitalized and subject to the same terms and
conditions as the principal. IDC on direct loans to ESCOM is assumed to
be paid as it accrues out of internally generated funds.

ANNEX 6.1

MALAWI
POWER V PROJECT
Least Cost Power Generation Expansion Program
Alternative Generation Options
1.          Following completion of the Tedzani III hydro-electric scheme in
1994, and assuming demand growth follows the Base case forecast, ESCOM will
require an additional source of power supply in 1997. The options available
are: (a) a new hydroelectric plant on the Shire river, the only water course
with significant hydro potential; (b) a gas turbine, diesel or coal-fired
thermal power plant; or (c) imports from neighboring Mozambique or Zambia.
2.          Imports from Mozambique or Zambia are not an economically-viable
option. While there is likely to be surplus capacity available in 1997 from
the Cahora Bassa power station in Mozambique, this capacity is fully committed
to the Republic of South Africa from about the year 2000. This short three-
year period of availability would not justify the construction of a high-
voltage transmission link between Cahora Bassa and the ESCOM power system.
Although Zambia currently has surplus power generating capacity, this capacity
is fully contracted to Zimbabwe through the mid-1990s. Based on the long
history of cooperation between Zambia and Zimbabwe in power supply, this
contract is likely to ba extended through the end of the century, by which
time the Zambian surplus is forecast to be fully absorbed. Consequently there
is no significant potential for export from Zambia to Malawi in 1997 or the
years immediately thereafter.
3.          The alternative of diesel generation capacity would cost about
US$1200/kW to install and gas turbine capacity about US$500/kW at 1990 price
level. This is less than the capital cost of new hydro electric plant,
estimated to be about US$1500/kW. However, reliance on the option of diesel
or gas - fired plant for base load purposes is ruled out by the high cost of
imported fuel in Malawi. At 1991 prices, this results in an operating cost of
USc13/kWh for diesel and almost USc3O for a gas turbine plant, more than 4 to
10 times that of alternative hydro-electric facilities.
4.          Coal fired thermal generation is similarly not an economic option.
Malawi has very limited proven reserves of low-quality coal which are not
sufficient to fuel a coal-fired thermal power station of significant size.
The least-cost source of imported coal is the Moatize mine in Mozambique.
Coal from this source currently costs about US$65/tonne delivered to Blantyre.
At this price, coal-fired generation would cost about three times that of
hydrogeneration. In addition, the unstable security situation in Mozambique
means that delivery from Moatize is highly unreliable. Malawi has therefore
wisely decided not to base its power generation expansion program on coal
supply from its source.   In light of the above, development of hydro-electric
facilities on the Shire river is clearly the least-cost option for meeting
Malawi's future power generation needs. A thorough review of the alternative
potential sites for new hydro stations to follow the commissioning of Tedzani



- 109 -
ANNEX 6.1
Page 2 of 2
III has been carried out for ESCOM by the US consulting firm of TAMS.1/ The
resulting estimated cost per kW installed of the leading candidate sites was
as follows:
Site/Protect                         CaDacity Cost
(K/kW)       ($/kW)
Kapichira                          1058         705
Kholombidzo                        1157         771
Mpatamanga                         1350         900
5.          This analysis confirmed that Kapichira is the least-cost option,
with a cost nearly 10% less than that of Kholombidzo, the next least-cost
alternative. The results were tested for sensitivity to the possible
construction of a regulating dam at Kholombidzo, which would increase the
effective capacity of both potential stations. It was concluded that this
would raise the potential cost saving from constructing Kapichira first from
just under 10% to 18%, thus reinforcing the case for proceeding with the
Kapichira project.

MALAWI
POWER V PROJECT
Economic Analysis of Salima - Lilongwe ° Chintheche
132 kV Transmission Line'
The Existing Line
1.    A 335 km 13kV wood pole transmission line connects Salima, via the
Lilongwe B substation, to Chintheche in northern Malawi. This line is
the sole means of bulk power supply from the generating stations in the
south to consumers in the north. It currently carries a load of 9 MW
that has been growing at over 7% per year. The line supplies a number
of sugar, tobacco, tea and coffee estates, an ethanol plant, a plywood
factory and several thousand domestic consumers. A paper factory, a
cement factory and further irrigation projects are under discussion.
The potential paper factory would be a large consumer, with demand of at
least 10 MW.
2.    The existing line was constructed in 1980-82.  Termite damage to
the wood poles began to be a problem in the late 1980s and has
progressed to the extent that the line is now routinely shut down for
planned pole replacement for one day every two -eeks. In addition,
unplanned outages due to termite damage are currently averaging 120
hours per year and rising by about 10% per year.
Ontions for ImRrovinR Reliability
3.    Three options for overcoming the line's reliability problems have
been considered. One option would be to install diesel generators in
the load centers served by the line. The initial investment required
would be about US$7.5 million, and operating costs would average
KO.9/kWh about six times the tariff. Parts of the line would still need
to be used to transmit power, which would not eliminate the reliability
problem. This option is therefore neither a least-cost nor effective
solution. The other two options are to rehabilitate the existing line
by replacing the wood poles with steel towers or to construct a new
steel tower line. While marginally cheaper in capital cost terms, the
rehabilitation option would require the line to be out of service for
long periods during the construction phase. This would impose high
costs on consumers, which renders the option uneconomic. Replacement of
the line with a new line constructed with steel towers is therefore the
least cost option.
Economic Justification of a New Line
4.    The economic justification for constructing a new line was
confirmed by estimating the project's economic rate of return. The
project's costs consist of the necessary capital expenditure, with
totals K43.4 million spread over four years. Routine maintenance was
assumed to be similar to that of the existing line and excluded from the
analysis. The project's benefits are the planned and unplanned outages



- 111 -    ANNEX 6.2
Page 2 of 3
avoided by installing the new line. Planned outages avoided are
conservatively assumed to remain constant at the current rate of 24
hours per two week period over the economic life of the line, which is
taken to be 25 years. Unplanned outages avoided are assumed to increase
by 5% per year over the economic life of the line. The current load of
9 MW is assumed to grow by 7% per year, slightly below the historic
trend. A 65% load factor, the system average, is used. The cost of
power outages to consumers is extremely difficult to estimate
accurately. Estimates for three of ESCOM's largest consumers range from
KO.6/kWh to K1.6/kWh in 1989 prices. For this analysis, an estimate of
RO.9/kWh was used, which is below the mid-point of the estimated range
of outage costs and the cost of diesel generation on the isolated
northern system. Based on this value of avoided outages, the project
has an economic rate of return of 12%.

AM 6.4

POWER V PROJECT
Estimation of the Economic Rate of Return
Project Definiiton
1.      The economic rate of return (ERR) has been estimated for the power
system investment and technical assistance components of the project combined,
plus units 3* 4 and 5 of the Kapichira hydro-electric station, which will be
installed later and funded outside the project. The rationale for including
the costs and benefits of these three units is that they are an integral part
of the Kapichira scheme.
Period of Analysis
2.      The analysis covers the period 1992-2019.  This was selected for
consistency with the projected economic life of the shortest-lived project
components, which are the transmission and distribution reinforcement
investments. It is assumed that the Kapichira hydroelectric scheme has a
longer economic life of 50 years, and net benefits are estimated and included
in the analysis for this 50 year period.
Economic Costs
3.      The economic costs used in the analysis consist of: (a) the estimated
capital cost of the project's power componentst plus (b) the capital cost of
generating units 3, 4 and 5 of the Kapichira power plant; plus (c) the
estimated operations and maintenance cost of the total package of capital
investments, which are calculated at 2% of the investment cost. The costs of
Units 3, 4 and 5 at Kapichira are included because the scheme is an integrated
facility, the physical structure and first two units of which will be financed
under the project, the last three units financed separately and installed
later as justified by demand. Operations and maintenance costs for Kapichira
from 2019, the assumed economic life of the transmission and distribution
assets, to 2043, the assumed economic life of the Kapichira hydro plant, are
discounted to 2019 at a rate of 10%. All costs are in 1990 constant prices.
Economic Benefits
4.      Economic benefits are estimated for:  (a) the generation
rehabilitation, transmission and distribution reinforcement components; and
(b) the Kapichira hydro-electric scheme, and then summed to give total project
benefits. All benefits are expressed in terms of 1990 constant prices.
5.      With respect to the category (a) components, the benefits of
generation rehabilitation will consist of reduced diesel or gas turbine
generation and reduced outages; the benefits of the transmission line
replacement will consist of reduced outages; and the benefits of the
distribution reinforcement program will consist of reduced outages and



- 115 -                   ANNEX 6.4
Page 2 of 3
additional power supply. The economic value of these benefits will range from
a minimum of the future tariff (t15.5/kWh) in the case of additional supply to
a maximum of the cost of substitute private generation (up to R3/kWh) in the
case of avoided outages. The cost of avoided diesel generation on the
interconnected system (90.9/kWh) represents a realistic average value of
these benefits and is used in the analysis. The estimated size of the
benefits stream in terms of GWh is based on the results of the loss reduction
study, the transmission line analysis (Annex 6.2) and the forecast reduction
in diesel generation from rehabilitating the older hydro-electric stations.
In combination, these benefits are estimated to total 1% of generation in
1993, 3% of generation in 1904, 5% iu 1995 and 6% in 1996. The benefits are
held constant at this level through 2019, the assumed economic life of the
related investments.
6.      The economic benefits of the Kapichira hydro-electric scheme are the
forecast incremental sales made possible by commissioning the station and by
the subsequent installation of units 3, 4, and 5. These benefits are assumed
to begin in 1998 and to rise progressively to 2005, when it is forecast that
the station's firm energy capability will be fully utilized. As the benefits
stream is based on firm, not average energy capability, it is inherently
conservative and almost certainly understated. The benefits are valued at
tlS.5/kWh, the forecast 1998 tariff, deflated to 1990. This represents a
minimum value of the benefits, as revealed by consumers' willingness to pay.
The actual value of benefits will range from thin minimum level up to the cost
of alternative generation which, for a small diesel plant, is in the range of
Kl-K3/kWh. Benefits over the scheme's second 25 years of its assumed 50 year
economic life are discounted to 2019 at a rate of 10%.
7.      Based on the above assumptions, the project's economic rate of return
is estimated to be 16.1%.
Risks and Sensitivity Analysis
8.      The principal risks of the project are:  (a) underestimation of the
costs of the Kapichira hydro scheme; (b) overestimation of demand growth; and
(c) delays in the construction of Kapichira. The generation rehabilitation
transmission and distribution components all involve relatively simple and
standard technologies and are not considered subject to any significant
technical risk.
9.      The sensitivity of the economic rate of return to a possible cost
overrun on Kapichira was tested by assuming that capital costs are
underestimated by a factor of 20% and benefii:a are unchanged. The resulting
estimated project ERR falls to 14.0%. The impact of a possible overestimation
of demand growth in the ERR was tested by assuming that demand grows 20% less
than the base case demand scenario. This results in an estimated ERR of
12.2%. A delay in the completion of Kapichira would raise both its capital
cost and also its utilization (and hence benefits) in the years immediately
following completion. Assuming that tariffs would be raised to cover the
increased capital costs and that demand is relatively inelastic, the resulting
ERR would be above the 16.1% base case estimate.
